#### Overview of vectors
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=0)** - We've reached the point in this course where we'll need to start using math to describe what's happening in our quantum circuits.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=7)** In case you're not familiar with basic linear algebra and how to work with vectors and complex numbers, this video and the next one will give you a quick overview of the basic concepts you'll need to get started.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=18)** In this video, we'll start with how to define a vector and visualize it on a graph using Cartesian coordinates.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=25)** Then we'll look at how to calculate the magnitude of a vector, scale it, and normalize it to a unit length of one.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=32)** We'll see several common vector operations, including addition, transpose, and inner product.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=38)** And finally, we'll explore the concept of an orthonormal basis and what it means for two vectors to be linearly independent.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=46)** If you already understand those topics, feel free to skip over the rest of this video.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=51)** In mathematics, a vector is a set of values that represent a quantity that has a direction and magnitude within some vector space.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=60)** Vectors are typically written as a set of numbers enclosed in brackets as shown here.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=65)** This vector has two elements, so we say it represents a direction and magnitude within a two-dimensional vector space.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=73)** A vector with three elements lives in a three-dimensional vector space, four elements makes a four-dimensional vector, five is a five-dimensional vector, and so on.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=84)** The world we live in is only three-dimensional, so it's difficult for us to visualize things in four or more dimensions even though they easily exist in the world of mathematics.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=95)** So to avoid melting our brains trying to visualize huge vector spaces, let's stick with just two dimensions for now.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=102)** For example, consider this vector named a, written in lower case.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=107)** The notation for a variable that represents a vector can vary widely.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=111)** You might see vector variables written with an arrow or line overhead, or perhaps as an underlined variable.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=118)** In print, vectors are often indicated using boldface.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=121)** There are lots of ways to decorate the variable, but they all mean the same thing, a is a vector with these two values.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=129)** We can visualize that vector on a two-dimensional plane using what's known as a Cartesian coordinate system, where each of its elements represents a distance along one of the two axes.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=140)** The first value, three, represents a distance along the horizontal x-axis, and the second value, four, is a distance along the vertical y-axis.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=150)** If we draw a line from the origin or center of the graph to that point, we've made a vector.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=156)** It has a direction, in this case pointing up to the right, and a magnitude or length.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=162)** Let's place a second vector b with the elements negative one and negative three.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=167)** That gives us a shorter vector pointing downwards to the left.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=171)** Now vectors don't always have to start with their tail at the center of the origin.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=176)** These vectors could be located here or even here or here.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=180)** It doesn't matter where we move those vectors within our two-dimensional space, they retain their same direction and magnitude.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=187)** That said, you'll usually see vectors drawn with their tail starting at the origin.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=193)** Now the direction of these two vectors should be fairly obvious because we've drawn them as arrows, but we also learned that vectors have a magnitude.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=201)** So what does that mean?
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=203)** The term magnitude refers to the length of a vector and is often denoted using this double vertical bar notation around the variable name.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=212)** To calculate the magnitude of a vector, we square each of its individual elements, add them all together, and then calculate the square root of that total.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=221)** The equation shown here would apply to a two-dimensional vector where a1 indicates the first element and a2 is the second element.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=230)** If we had a three-dimensional vector, we would square and add all three of its elements before taking the square root.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=236)** For four dimensions, we'd square and add all four together, and so on.
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=240)** You may hear calculating the magnitude this way referred to as the Euclidean norm, but for this course, we'll simply use the term magnitude.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=249)** Now let's apply that equation to calculate the magnitude of our two-dimensional vector a.
>
> **[4:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=255)** Three squared is nine and four squared is 16.
>
> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=258)** Adding those squares together gives us 25 and the square root of that is five.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=263)** So this vector a has a magnitude or length of five.
>
> **[4:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=267)** Applying the same formula to vector b gives us the square root of 10, which is an irrational number that equals a little more than three.
>
> **[4:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=275)** Notice that even though the values in vector b are negative, the magnitude is still positive due to the equation squaring all of its values.
>
> **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=285)** Whether a vector's elements are positive or negative will affect its direction but not its magnitude.
>
> **[4:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=291)** That covers the basics of how we represent and visualize vectors with a direction and magnitude.
>
> **[4:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=297)** Now let's talk about some of the operations we can apply to vectors.
>
> **[5:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=302)** When we multiply a vector by a single numeric value, which is referred to as a scalar, that will change the magnitude of the vector but not its direction.
>
> **[5:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=311)** We've scaled the length of the vector.
>
> **[5:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=314)** For example, using our vector a with the elements three and four, if we multiply that by two, that doubles both of its values.
>
> **[5:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=323)** So two times three gives us six and two times four is eight.
>
> **[5:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=328)** Applying the magnitude formula to that scaled vector, 2a, shows that it has a length of 10.
>
> **[5:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=334)** We've doubled the magnitude of a, but its direction is unchanged.
>
> **[5:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=339)** While we're on the topic of scaling vectors, let's talk about a related operation known as normalization, which scales a vector to have a magnitude of one which is considered the standard or normal length for a vector, often referred to as the unit length.
>
> **[5:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=354)** The equation to normalize a vector is shown here, simply dividing each of the elements of the vector by its overall magnitude.
>
> **[6:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=362)** Don't worry about remembering that formula for now.
>
> **[6:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=364)** What you need to understand for this course is the concept that a normalized vector has a mathematical length or magnitude of one pointing in some direction.
>
> **[6:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=374)** In addition to scaling vectors, we can also add multiple vectors together as long as those vectors have the same number of elements.
>
> **[6:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=382)** For example, we can sum vectors a and b together to produce a new vector by adding together each of their corresponding values.
>
> **[6:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=390)** Adding together the first elements, three plus negative one, gives us two, and adding the second elements, four plus negative three, gives us one.
>
> **[6:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=399)** So the sum of vectors a and b is a vector with the values two and one.
>
> **[6:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=405)** We can visually represent that addition operation on a graph by drawing our first vector a with its tail at the origin and then drawing the second vector b with its tail starting at the head of a.
>
> **[6:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=416)** Drawing a vector from the origin to the end of that summed together chain of a and b illustrates the resulting sum, the vector two, one.
>
> **[7:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=425)** Another important vector operation we'll need to use is the transpose operation, which takes a column vector whose elements are arranged vertically in a column and flips it to become a row vector where the elements are arranged horizontally.
>
> **[7:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=439)** And if you apply the transpose operation to a row vector, that flips it back up into a column vector.
>
> **[7:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=446)** The transpose operation is denoted with the letter T as a raised superscript above a variable.
>
> **[7:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=452)** So for example, if a is our column vector with the elements three and four, a transpose is the row vector with elements three and four.
>
> **[7:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=462)** Now it may not seem like we're doing anything useful here since the individual elements are unchanged, but the transpose does change the vector's dimensions, which matters when we start multiplying vectors and matrices.
>
> **[7:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=474)** And that brings us to our next operation, the inner product.
>
> **[7:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=478)** The inner product is a way to multiply two vectors together such that they produce a single scalar value as the result.
>
> **[8:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=486)** This operation requires both vectors to have the same number of elements.
>
> **[8:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=490)** For vector multiplication to produce a scalar result, the left vector needs to be oriented as a row vector and the right vector needs to be a column.
>
> **[8:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=500)** So when we multiply these three-dimensional vectors, a, b, c, and x, y, z, we multiply the first element of each vector, which is a times x, then we sum that with the product of the second elements, b and y, along with the corresponding products of any other elements, in this case that's c times z.
>
> **[8:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=521)** This expression, ax plus by plus cz, results in a single scalar value.
>
> **[8:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=528)** Symbolically, the inner product operation is often denoted using the small dot symbols shown here.
>
> **[8:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=535)** For that reason, you may hear the inner product referred to as a dot product.
>
> **[8:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=539)** There are some subtle distinctions between those terms, but they're not relevant to the way we'll be using them here.
>
> **[9:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=546)** So if we want to take the inner product of our two example column vectors from earlier in this video, a and b, we would write that as a dot b.
>
> **[9:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=556)** Now, since those are both column vectors, to multiply them to produce a scalar, we'll need to transpose vector a, flipping it on its side to become a row vector.
>
> **[9:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=566)** When we multiply and sum the corresponding terms from the vectors, that gives us an inner product of negative 15.
>
> **[9:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=573)** Now that we've seen how to scale, normalize, sum, transpose, and multiply vectors, there's one more vector-related concept we need to cover and that's something called an orthonormal basis.
>
> **[9:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=586)** Fancy words.
>
> **[9:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=587)** Let's break them down.
>
> **[9:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=589)** The term basis refers to a set of vectors that can be linearly combined to form any other vector within that space.
>
> **[9:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=596)** The term linear combination means we're using addition and multiplication to scale and add vectors together like we saw earlier, thus combining them.
>
> **[10:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=605)** The term orthonormal means our vectors are orthogonal or perpendicular to each other and have a unit length of one.
>
> **[10:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=613)** Using our two-dimensional space as an example, the vectors one, zero and zero, one would form an orthonormal basis because they both have a length of one, they're perpendicular to each other, and, by linearly combining these two vectors using addition and multiplication, we can scale and add them to produce any other vector within this two-dimensional space.
>
> **[10:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=636)** For example, we can represent our example vector a from earlier as three times the vector one, zero plus four times the vector zero, one.
>
> **[10:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=645)** That linear combination gives us a, which is three, four.
>
> **[10:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=650)** The other example, vector b, can also be expressed as a linear combination of these same two basis vectors.
>
> **[10:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=657)** However, it is not possible to express one of these basis vectors in terms of the other one because they're orthogonal.
>
> **[11:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=666)** The term linearly independent is used to describe when a vector cannot be expressed as a linear combination of one or more other vectors in the space.
>
> **[11:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=675)** That applies to our two orthogonal basis vectors because there's no way to scale and add together that one, zero vector to produce the zero, one vector and vice versa.
>
> **[11:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=686)** Additionally, if we calculate the inner product of those two vectors, the result will be zero because they're linearly independent.
>
> **[11:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=694)** Now, the vectors one, zero and zero, one are not the only possible orthonormal basis for a two-dimensional vector space.
>
> **[11:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=702)** For example, these two diagonal vectors are also orthogonal and normalized and could therefore be used in the same way as a basis pair that can be linearly combined to create any other vector.
>
> **[11:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=715)** Really, any pair of orthogonal and normalized vectors can serve as a basis in two-dimensional vector space.
>
> **[12:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=722)** However, the set one, zero and zero, one is by far the simplest and most common.
>
> **[12:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=727)** And as we'll see soon, these two orthonormal vectors are used to represent the two standard basis states for quantum bits.
>
> **[12:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=736)** Wow, we just covered a lot in this video, and if you feel like some parts are still unclear, that's okay.
>
> **[12:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=744)** Rather than trying to perfectly understand everything about vectors now, I recommend continuing on.
>
> **[12:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=749)** These concepts and operations should start to make more sense as you see them used in action.
>
> **[12:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-vectors?u=76281980&t=754)** And if later you still find yourself struggling with the vector math, remember, you can always come back to this video as a reference.

#### Overview of complex numbers
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=0)** - Like the previous video on vectors, this video will be a quick overview of complex numbers.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=6)** So if you're already comfortable working with complex numbers in standard and polar forms, as well as performing operations like the complex conjugate, feel free to skip past this video.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=18)** Now to understand complex numbers, we first need to understand the concepts of real and imaginary numbers.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=25)** A real number is any value that can be represented as a distance along a line.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=30)** For example, 1, 2, and 3 are all real numbers, as are their negative counterparts going in the other direction.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=39)** Real numbers can be fractional values, like 1/4 or -1.78, and they can even be irrational numbers, like square root of 2 or pi, which require an infinite number of decimal places to fully represent.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=52)** Real numbers are quantities that can exist in the real world that we live in.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=56)** They're the type of values that we use in everyday life.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=59)** For example, I have three apples.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=61)** That's a real number representing a real quantity of apples I can physically have.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=66)** Imaginary numbers, on the other hand, are a mathematical concept to express values that cannot be represented in the real world.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=74)** Centuries ago, philosophers and mathematicians considered the question: What is the square root of -1, or put in another way, what number can be squared or multiplied by itself to produce the value -1?
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=87)** This seemingly simple equation is impossible to solve using real numbers because, when you square any real number, positive or negative, the result will always be a positive value.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=99)** Since this type of value doesn't exist in the world of real numbers, we use the term imaginary number to describe a number that produces a negative result when squared.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=109)** Imaginary numbers are usually written as a real number multiplied by the imaginary unit, which we'll represent with the lowercase letter i.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=118)** This imaginary unit is defined such that i squared is equal to -1.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=124)** It's impossible to represent an imaginary quantity in the real world.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=128)** I can't have square root of -1 apples.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=131)** There's no such thing.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=132)** We can't visualize that with physical objects.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=135)** However, in the world of mathematics, imaginary numbers play a very real role within the mathematical frameworks we use to describe the world around us.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=144)** So we need to get comfortable seeing and using them.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=148)** Now that we've defined real and imaginary numbers, we can return to the original topic of the video, complex numbers.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=155)** A complex number is a value consisting of both a real and imaginary component.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=160)** We can write a complex number, which we'll refer to with the variable z, as a plus b times i, where a and b are both real values, and i is the imaginary unit.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=171)** The value a represents the real part of the complex number, and b is the imaginary part.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=176)** For example, the complex number shown here has a real component of 3 and an imaginary component of 2, which is multiplied by the imaginary value i.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=186)** Keep in mind, 3 and 2 are both real numbers, but we refer to 2 as the imaginary component because it gets multiplied by i.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=195)** Another way to represent this complex number is in the form of an ordered pair, or a vector, where the first element is the real component, and the second element is the imaginary component.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=206)** Since a complex number consists of two real numbers, we can visually represent it as a vector on a two-dimensional plane.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=213)** We'll use the Cartesian coordinate system with the horizontal axis representing the real component and the vertical axis representing the imaginary component.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=222)** So our complex number 3 + 2i would be drawn as this vector on our complex plane.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=228)** A number having a real value of -1 and no imaginary component would be a vector along the horizontal real axis, and a purely imaginary number with no real component, the point along the vertical imaginary access.
>
> **[4:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=242)** We just saw some examples of writing complex numbers in the form of a + bi and drawing them on a complex plane using Cartesian coordinates.
>
> **[4:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=251)** But we can also represent complex numbers in another format, with polar coordinates.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=256)** We can write a complex number in the polar form as r times e raised to the power of i times theta.
>
> **[4:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=262)** In this expression, r and theta are both real numbers.
>
> **[4:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=266)** and e is Euler's number, which is also a real number which is the value of around 2.7.
>
> **[4:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=272)** When we use polar coordinates to draw that complex value as a vector on our two-dimensional plane, theta represents the angle of the vector relative to the positive real axis in units of radians.
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=283)** In other words, theta determines which direction the vector is pointing.
>
> **[4:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=287)** You'll often hear theta referred to as the phase angle of this complex number.
>
> **[4:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=292)** The other variable, r, represents the magnitude or length of the vector.
>
> **[4:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=297)** That gives us two different ways to write a complex number and visualize it in a two-dimensional plane.
>
> **[5:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=303)** We have the standard form, a + bi, and the polar form, r times e to the power of i times theta.
>
> **[5:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=310)** Notice that both of these expressions contain two real numbers, a and b in the standard form and r and theta in the polar form.
>
> **[5:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=318)** That said, a and b and r and theta are not the same.
>
> **[5:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=322)** So how do they relate to each other?
>
> **[5:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=325)** To bridge the gap between these two formats, we'll need to use Euler's formula.
>
> **[5:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=329)** Euler's formula states that e raised to the value of i times theta is equal to the cosine of theta plus i times the sine of theta.
>
> **[5:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=338)** We won't go into how Leonhard Euler came up with the equation or his famous number.
>
> **[5:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=342)** For now, we'll just accept it as an equation that's used throughout modern mathematics.
>
> **[5:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=348)** Looking at the left half of the equation, notice it's similar to the expression we use to represent a complex number in polar form, but now it's missing the scalar r.
>
> **[5:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=357)** So let's multiply both sides of that equation by a real magnitude, r, and then on the right side of the equation, we'll use algebra to distribute r to the both parts.
>
> **[6:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=368)** Now the left side of the equation is the polar form of a complex number that we saw earlier, and the right side has two parts: r times cosine theta represents the real component of the imaginary number, and r times i times sine theta is the imaginary part.
>
> **[6:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=384)** Together, those two parts represent our standard format for a complex number of a plus b times i.
>
> **[6:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=392)** To help make that more concrete, let's put some actual numbers behind that equation.
>
> **[6:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=397)** Let's make the magnitude r equal to 3 and the phase angle theta be pi/4.
>
> **[6:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=403)** The expression with the cosine evaluates to 3 over square root of 2.
>
> **[6:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=407)** This represents the real component of our complex number.
>
> **[6:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=410)** And the other part, with sine of pi/4, represents the imaginary part.
>
> **[6:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=416)** Side by side, we can see how the pairs of real numbers used to describe a complex value are different, depending on whether we're using the polar form or the standard form.
>
> **[7:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=426)** Now you might be wondering, why do we need two different formats to represent the exact same complex number?
>
> **[7:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=433)** Wouldn't it be simpler to just stick with one format?
>
> **[7:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=436)** Well when doing mathematical manipulations, some things are easier to do using the standard form, and some things are easier to do in the polar form.
>
> **[7:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=445)** We'll be using both representations at various points throughout this course depending on the concept we're trying to explain.
>
> **[7:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=452)** So you need to be familiar with what those variables mean in both formats.
>
> **[7:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=456)** Now there's a few more concepts we need to cover related to complex numbers.
>
> **[7:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=462)** First, we'll need to know how to find the absolute value of a complex number.
>
> **[7:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=466)** When visualized on the complex plane, the absolute value of a complex number is its distance from the origin.
>
> **[7:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=473)** If we've written our complex number using the polar form, then finding the absolute value is easy.
>
> **[7:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=478)** It's just the magnitude value of r.
>
> **[8:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=480)** If we expressed our complex number in the standard form, a plus b times i, then we can use this equation, which Baron introduced in the previous video, to calculate the magnitude of that vector.
>
> **[8:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=492)** Knowing how to find the absolute value of a complex number will become important when we calculate the probability of getting different outcomes when measuring quantum states.
>
> **[8:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=501)** Another operation we'll need is the complex conjugate.
>
> **[8:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=504)** The conjugate of a complex number is the similar complex number, but with the sign of the imaginary component flipped from positive to negative or vice versa.
>
> **[8:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=514)** The complex conjugate is often denoted in physics with a superscript star.
>
> **[8:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=518)** In terms of the standard a + b form, we can think of the complex conjugate as flipping the sign on b.
>
> **[8:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=525)** In the polar form, the conjugate negates the exponent.
>
> **[8:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=529)** It's important to recognize that a complex number has the same magnitude as its conjugate, so the magnitude of a complex number z is equal to the magnitude of its complex conjugate.
>
> **[9:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=540)** Now that we've seen how to conjugate complex numbers, we're ready for our final operation, the conjugate transpose.
>
> **[9:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=547)** Finding the conjugate transpose of a vector is a two-step process that involves transposing a vector and applying the complex conjugate to each of its elements.
>
> **[9:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=556)** There are several different symbols that are commonly used to represent this operation.
>
> **[9:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=561)** In the context of quantum mechanics, we use the dagger symbol as a superscript.
>
> **[9:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=566)** The order in which we apply the transpose and conjugate operations doesn't matter.
>
> **[9:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=570)** We can conjugate all the elements first then transpose the vector, or vice versa.
>
> **[9:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=575)** The result will always be the same.
>
> **[9:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=577)** For example, consider the column vector a shown here.
>
> **[9:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=581)** Its conjugate transpose, a dagger, is a row vector where the sign of the imaginary components have been flipped.
>
> **[9:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=588)** You might also hear the conjugate transpose operation referred to as the Hermitian conjugate or Hermitian transpose.
>
> **[9:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=595)** Those are just other names for it.
>
> **[9:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=597)** It's also worth noting that we've only considered vectors thus far, but this conjugate transpose operation also applies to matrices.
>
> **[10:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-complex-numbers?u=76281980&t=605)** But that's a topic we'll cover later in this course.

#### Represent qubits as vectors
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=0)** - It can be difficult to form an intuitive understanding of quantum computing because the tiny world of quantum mechanics works differently than the way we experience the world as humans.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=11)** And that's why math is an important tool for understanding many of these quantum concepts.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=16)** We'll be using linear algebra and other abstract models to describe quantum computations throughout this course.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=22)** So let's take a few minutes to look at how a qubit, which is our fundamental unit of quantum information, can be represented mathematically.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=31)** A quantum bit has two computational basis states, which we'll call zero and one.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=37)** These two basis states are mutually exclusive and can be represented by a pair of two dimensional vectors.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=43)** The zero state is a vector with one as its first element and zero as its second element, and the one state is the opposite of that, with one as the second element, and zero as the first.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=55)** These two vectors are linearly independent, and together they form an ortho normal basis.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=61)** Now, you might have noticed this funny looking symbol with a vertical bar and angle bracket.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=67)** That symbol is part of Dirac notation, which is used to describe quantum mechanical states.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=72)** We use it to indicate that zero and one are quantum states, and not classical bit values, or just the numbers zero in one.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=81)** This symbol with a variable enclosed between a vertical bar and right pointing angle bracket is called a ket, and it specifically denotes a quantum state that's represented as a column vector.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=93)** So, you may sometimes hear our two standard basis states referred to as ket-zero and ket-one.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=99)** Now, how do we take those two states, ket-zero and one, and use them to describe a quantum state that can be both zero and one at the same time?
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=109)** - Simple.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=110)** A quantum state is just a linear combination of those computational basis states in a complex vector space.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=116)** - That was a lot of words.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=117)** Would you mind breaking that down?
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=119)** - Sure. You'll often see the Greek simple si used in a ket to represent an arbitrary quantum state.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=126)** That quantum state is a linear combination of some scaler value alpha, times ket-zero, plus another scaler value beta, times ket-one.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=135)** Another way to write this expression is to replace cat zero and one with their corresponding vectors.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=142)** If we then scale those vectors and send them together that gives us a single two-dimensional vector with alpha as a first element and beta as a second element.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=152)** These are just different ways to write a quantum state using Dirac notation or vector notation, and you'll see us use both throughout this course.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=160)** Now, it's important to note that alpha and beta are not just regular numbers, they're both complex numbers, meaning they each have a real and an imaginary component.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=169)** Why alpha and beta are complex numbers gets into details of quantum mechanics that are beyond the scope of this course, but if we can just accept that they're complex numbers and treat them as such the mathematical operations we'll be using throughout this course will work just fine.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=184)** - Those two coefficient values, alpha and beta, are called probability amplitudes.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=190)** They indicate how likely we are to get each of the two possible outcomes when measuring a qubit.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=196)** The absolute value of alpha squared represents the probability that the measurement outcome will be in the zero state, and the absolute value of beta squared is the probability that the outcome will be in the one state.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=208)** These two amplitudes themselves, alpha and beta, can be positive or negative, because they're just complex values.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=215)** For example, alpha could be negative one over the square root of two.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=220)** However, when we use that amplitude to calculate the probability of measuring the zero state, the probability will always be positive, because we're squaring the absolute value of alpha.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=232)** One important rule of probability is that the individual probabilities for all possible outcomes must always add up to one.
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=240)** That puts a constraint on the values alpha and beta that the sum of the squares of their absolute values must always equal one.
>
> **[4:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=248)** This is often referred to as the normalization constraint, because it means the two-dimensional vector that alpha and beta represent will have a normalized unit length of one.
>
> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=258)** We can visualize that by plotting the quantum state vector on a simplified, two-dimensional graph with the alpha probability amplitude on the vertical axis and beta on the horizontal axis.
>
> **[4:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=269)** Even though alpha and beta are actually complex numbers, to keep this example simple, we'll restrict them both to being only real positive numbers, but the concepts are the same.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=281)** If we draw the state vector for a qubit where alpha equals one and beta is zero, that's a line along the vertical axis which represents our ket-zero basis state.
>
> **[4:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=291)** To calculate the probability that will get an outcome of zero when measuring this quantum state, we'll square the absolute value of alpha, which gives us a probability of one.
>
> **[5:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=301)** If we do the same thing to calculate the probability of getting an outcome of one by squaring the absolute value of beta, that gives us zero.
>
> **[5:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=309)** This qubit is simply in the zero basis state.
>
> **[5:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=312)** No matter how many times we measure it, the outcome will always be zero.
>
> **[5:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=316)** If we swap those values for alpha and beta, now the line is on the horizontal axis, pointing to ket-one.
>
> **[5:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=324)** Now there's zero probability that measuring this qubit will give us the outcome of zero, because the outcome will always be one.
>
> **[5:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=331)** This qubit is simply in the one basis state.
>
> **[5:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=334)** Those two examples showed what the standard basis states look like on our graph, but things get more interesting when we put our qubit into super position
>
> **[5:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=343)** - When a quantum bit is in a super position of both zero and one at the same time.
>
> **[5:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=348)** That means both alpha and beta have non-zero values.
>
> **[5:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=351)** For example, in the quantum state shown here, alpha and beta are both one over the square root of two.
>
> **[5:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=358)** That's represented on our graph as a diagonal vector with a magnitude of one.
>
> **[6:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=362)** Squaring the absolute value of those two amplitudes tells us that the probability of measuring an outcome of zero is one half, and the probability of getting one is also one half.
>
> **[6:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=373)** So measuring a qubit in this quantum state is kind of like flipping a coin, with an equal probability of measuring one or zero.
>
> **[6:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=380)** Finally, consider the superposition state where the probability of measuring a zero outcome is higher than measuring a one.
>
> **[6:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=388)** If we had a handful of cubits that are all in the super position and measured each one, the majority of these measurements would likely collapse to zero.
>
> **[6:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=396)** However, we could still encounter measuring several ones along the way.
>
> **[6:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=401)** The key takeaway from all of this is that the quantum state of a qubit can be represented as a two-dimensional vector of complex numbers with a unit length of one, and the probability amplitudes, alpha and beta, correspond to the probability of measuring their respective outcomes.
>
> **[6:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=416)** - Now that we've explored the concepts of state vectors and complex amplitudes, let's revisit Dirac notation to see how it relates to probabilities and measurement.
>
> **[7:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=425)** Remember that we use the ket symbol with a bar and the right angle bracket to denote a quantum state that's a column vector.
>
> **[7:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=433)** The counterpart to a ket vector is called a bra, and we indicated using a similar symbol with the angle bracket pointing to the left.
>
> **[7:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=441)** A bra is a row vector, or more specifically, bra is the conjugate transpose of a ket.
>
> **[7:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=448)** For example, if we have a column vector ket-si, with some arbitrary amplitudes, alpha and beta, bra-si will be its conjugate transpose which is a row vector with the complex conjugates of alpha and beta.
>
> **[7:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=462)** Now, why is this useful to have notation to distinguish between these row and column vectors?
>
> **[7:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=467)** Well, it makes it easy to represent some common operations.
>
> **[7:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=471)** If we multiply a bra with a ket, that gives us their inner product, which is a single scaler value.
>
> **[7:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=477)** For example, if we multiply bra-zero times ket-zero, that represents the inner product of the zero basis state with itself.
>
> **[8:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=486)** The ones in the first position of those two vectors line up, and the product is one.
>
> **[8:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=491)** We get this result because bra-zero and ket-zero represent the same quantum state.
>
> **[8:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=496)** However, if we multiply bra-one times ket-zero, the ones in those two vectors do not line up anymore so their inner product is zero.
>
> **[8:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=505)** This tells us that these zero and one state vectors are orthogonal, which makes sense, because they are a pair of ortho normal basis states.
>
> **[8:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=513)** As a side note, when multiplying a bra and a ket, we'll often clean up the notation by combining the two bars in the middle into a single one.
>
> **[8:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=522)** This means the same thing, and we might refer to these together as bra-ket, which sounds like bracket.
>
> **[8:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=529)** If you are wondering where the terms bra and ket came from, there's your source of inspiration, and you'll often hear the the name bra-ket notation used to describe Dirac notation.
>
> **[8:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=539)** Now, let's see what happens if we multiply bra-zero by an arbitrary quantum state, ket-si.
>
> **[9:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=545)** The one in bra-zero lines up with the alpha term, so our inner product is just alpha.
>
> **[9:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=551)** If we perform a similar operation on ket-si, but using bra-one instead of bra-zero, now the one is in the other position, so our inner product is beta.
>
> **[9:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=562)** This demonstrates that taking the inner product of ket-si with either of the basis states gives us the corresponding probability amplitude.
>
> **[9:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=569)** As we saw earlier in this video, the absolute value squared of these amplitudes gives us the probability of measuring each outcome, and we can express that using Dirac notation as shown here.
>
> **[9:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-as-vectors?u=76281980&t=581)** Knowing how to represent quantum states as vectors and with Dirac notation and how to calculate the probability of measuring different outcomes are essential concepts to quantum computing.

> [!info]- Semantic Content

#### Represent qubits on the Bloch sphere
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=0)** - In the previous video, we visualized a quantum state vector on a two-dimensional graph that simplified things to explain the concepts, but it overlooked the fact that we're actually working with complex numbers.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=13)** Our probability amplitudes, alpha and beta, are both complex numbers, which means they each have real and imaginary components indicated here with the Re and Im notation.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=25)** That means to fully represent this quantum state, consisting of two complex numbers, we'll actually need four real-valued coefficients.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=34)** Trying to visualize something in four dimensions is kind of hard.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=38)** - Fortunately, we don't need to.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=40)** We can use a representation called the Bloch sphere, which lets us visualize a pure quantum state in three-dimensional space as a point on the surface of a sphere.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=49)** - Hold up.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=50)** How do we just go from needing four dimensions to only three?
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=54)** - We'll get to that soon in another video.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=56)** For now, just know there are certain quantum states that are physically indistinguishable from each other.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=62)** They look the same so that reduces the number of unique states we need to visualize.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=67)** By using some clever math to represent a quantum state using polar coordinates, we can reduce the number of dimensions from four to three.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=74)** - Are we about to derive some really complicated equations?
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=78)** - I'll spare you that math for now.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=80)** The good news is that we don't really need to fully understand how the equations are derived to use the Bloch sphere as a tool for visualizing quantum states.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=89)** Let's set up our three-dimensional space with two perpendicular axes named X and Y, which span the horizontal plane, and a vertical axis, Z, which is perpendicular or normal to the X-Y plane.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=102)** If you've done any sort of 3D modeling or advanced math, this should look familiar.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=107)** Next we'll draw our spheres centered within that three-dimensional space.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=111)** The sphere is normalized, meaning the radius or distance from the center of this sphere to any point on its surface has a unit length of one.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=120)** The point at the very top of this sphere where the Z-axis pokes through represents the ket zero state of our standard basis, and the opposite point down at the very bottom represents the ket one state.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=132)** Now using the graphical framework, we can visualize any pure quantum state as a point on the surface of the sphere.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=139)** For example, consider this quantum state ket psi, which is a superposition of our two standard basis states.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=147)** Since the Bloch sphere has a unit radius of one, the magnitude or length of that vector will always be one, so we only need two values to describe that point on the surface.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=158)** First is what's known as the polar angle, represented here by the symbol theta.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=163)** This indicates the angle in radians from the polar Z-axis to the vector representing our quantum state.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=170)** The second piece of information we need to describe that point is the azimuth angle, which is the angle of rotation around the Z-axis along the X-Y plane represented here with the Greek symbol phi.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=182)** Using polar coordinates, we can represent that quantum state or point on the sphere using this equation.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=189)** The alpha coefficient is equivalent to the cosine of theta over two and the beta coefficient is Euler's number e raised to the power of i times phi multiplied by a sine of theta over two.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=201)** Understanding how we derive this equation is beyond the scope of this course, and not necessary to use it.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=207)** If you want to understand where this equation came from, check out the Qiskit textbook, it has a great walkthrough.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=213)** - Now to demonstrate how that equation can represent alpha and beta, let's assign those two polar variables some actual values.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=221)** Let's say this quantum state has a polar angle theta of pi over four radians, and the azimuth angle phi is three pi over eight radians.
>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=231)** If we substitute those values for theta and phi into our equation and solve for alpha and beta, we see that the alpha term of cosine pi over eight equals approximately 0.93, which is a completely real number without any imaginary component.
>
> **[4:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=247)** The beta term on the other hand has a real component of roughly 0.15 and an imaginary component of 0.35.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=256)** Those two complex probability amplitudes can also be written using vector notation as shown here.
>
> **[4:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=262)** If we calculate the probability of observing each outcome when measuring this quantum state, we see there's about an 85% chance of getting to zero state and a 15% probability of measuring it as one.
>
> **[4:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=274)** As we should expect, if we add those two probabilities together, they total to one or 100%.
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=280)** - It's important to remember that the Bloch sphere is mapping quantum state vectors onto a three-dimensional space to help visualize it.
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=288)** When working in a Cartesian coordinate system, we typically think of the x-axis, y-axis and z-axis as being orthogonal to one another because they're perpendicular.
>
> **[4:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=297)** However that's not how the Bloch sphere works.
>
> **[5:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=300)** With the Bloch sphere, we are representing quantum states, and if we take any two states on opposite sides of the sphere along the same axis, those two state vectors will be orthonormal.
>
> **[5:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=310)** That means together they form a valid basis to represent any other quantum state.
>
> **[5:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=315)** - The surface of a sphere has an infinite number of points, so does that mean there are an infinite number of possible bases?
>
> **[5:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=322)** - Yeah though there are few common ones that we'll reference throughout this course.
>
> **[5:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=327)** Of course, at the top and bottom of the Z-axis, we have our standard basis states, zero and one, shown here along with their vector representations.
>
> **[5:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=336)** The two states at opposite ends of the X-axis form another common basis pair and are usually identified using the ket plus and ket minus symbols.
>
> **[5:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=345)** Finally the ends of the Y-axis are another common pair of states.
>
> **[5:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=349)** For this course, we'll identify them as ket plus i and ket minus i.
>
> **[5:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=354)** Though if you're looking elsewhere, you may see those two states represented with other symbols.
>
> **[5:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=359)** Those four common states around the equator of the Bloch sphere represent different superpositions that all have the same equal probability of collapsing to one or zero when measured in the computational basis along the Z-axis.
>
> **[6:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=373)** We're including their state vectors here for reference, but don't worry about trying to memorize all these states right now.
>
> **[6:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-qubits-on-the-bloch-sphere?u=76281980&t=378)** We'll see them plenty throughout this course.
