>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/state-vectors-and-bloch-spheres-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=85)** Finally, let's comment out the last two lines in the second notebook cell so the circuit doesn't do anything other than initialize a fresh qubit.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/state-vectors-and-bloch-spheres-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=95)** Now I'll click the run all arrow button at the top to run this entire notebook, and at the end we can see the resulting state vector for our qubit.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/state-vectors-and-bloch-spheres-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=105)** Notice that Python uses the letter J to represent the imaginary unit which we've been representing with the letter I.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/state-vectors-and-bloch-spheres-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=112)** The first coefficient alpha has a real component of one and zero imaginary component and the second coefficient beta is zero for both.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/state-vectors-and-bloch-spheres-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=121)** So this qubit is in the initial cat zero basis state.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/state-vectors-and-bloch-spheres-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=125)** To make things easier to read, at the top of this notebook I'll import a function called array to latex from Qiskit's visualization package.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/state-vectors-and-bloch-spheres-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=135)** I'll swap out the print function at the end for array to latex.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/state-vectors-and-bloch-spheres-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=143)** Then click the run all arrow again and now our state vector is displayed with easy to read latex format.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/state-vectors-and-bloch-spheres-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=151)** Now to visualize this qubit on the bloch sphere, we'll need to import another function from the visualization module called plot bloch multi-vector.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/state-vectors-and-bloch-spheres-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=162)** Then down in the fourth notebook cell, we'll call the plot bloch multi-vector function and pass its state vector as the input argument.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/state-vectors-and-bloch-spheres-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=176)** I'll click the run all button again to execute those changes.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/state-vectors-and-bloch-spheres-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=180)** And now in addition to printing the state vector, Qiskit gives us a nice visualization of that quantum state on the bloch sphere.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/state-vectors-and-bloch-spheres-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=188)** Since the cubit is initialized to the cat zero state, the state vector is a narrow pointing straight up along the Z-axis.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/state-vectors-and-bloch-spheres-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=195)** Now let's apply an operation to change that qubit state to something else.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/state-vectors-and-bloch-spheres-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=200)** We'll un-comment the Hadamard Gate to add it back to the end of our quantum circuit.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/state-vectors-and-bloch-spheres-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=204)** Click the run all arrow again and now the bloch sphere has a horizontal vector pointing along the X axis.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/state-vectors-and-bloch-spheres-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=212)** Looking at the state vector above that, we can see that in this quantum state the alpha and beta coefficients both have a real component value of one over square root of two.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/state-vectors-and-bloch-spheres-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=222)** Since we removed the line of code to measure the qubit, the last operation in the circuit before it gets simulated is the Hadamard Gate which puts it into this superposition.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/state-vectors-and-bloch-spheres-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=232)** Now let's add the final measurement step back into our circuit by un-commenting it.
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/state-vectors-and-bloch-spheres-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=237)** I'll press the run all button to rerun that simulation.
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/state-vectors-and-bloch-spheres-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=240)** Now we're looking at the state of that qubit after it was measured.
>
> **[4:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/state-vectors-and-bloch-spheres-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=244)** So it was put into superposition then measured, which causes it to collapse to one of the basis states.
>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/state-vectors-and-bloch-spheres-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=250)** This time the measured outcome was one.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/state-vectors-and-bloch-spheres-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=253)** If I run that simulation several more times, sometimes the end-state will be zero and other times it will end up as one.
>
> **[4:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/state-vectors-and-bloch-spheres-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=260)** Qiskit's state vector simulator and bloch sphere visualization are useful tools to investigate what's going on with your circuits as you continue to explore and learn about quantum programming.

#### Global and relative phase
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=0)** - In the previous videos we saw how to visualize quantum states on the Bloch sphere.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=5)** But a pure quantum state is represented by two complex values, alpha and beta, which each have real and imaginary components.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=14)** That means four real numbers in total.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=16)** So how do we go from something that should take four dimensions to represent to only three dimensions on the Bloch sphere?
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=23)** Short answer, some of those quantum states look the same.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=27)** We've already seen the standard zero basis state shown here, represented as the vector one zero.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=33)** If we measure a qubit in that state the probability of getting an outcome of zero is the absolute value of one squared, which is one.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=41)** We will always measure this state as zero and we will never get an outcome of one.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=46)** Now, consider what happens if we negate that state or multiply it by negative one.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=52)** Now it's vector representation has a negative one in the first position and zero in the second.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=57)** But if we calculate the probability of each outcome when measuring that qubit, we see that the absolute value of negative one squared is also one.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=66)** So just like its original form, the negative version of the state will also always be measured as zero.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=73)** For one more example, let's see what happens if we multiply that state by the imaginary unit i.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=79)** That means the first element is i and the second element is still zero.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=83)** When we calculate the outcome probabilities for that state, again, we see that it will always be measured as zero.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=89)** What we have here are three quantum states that may mathematically look different from each other, but when we measure qubits in those states there's no way to physically tell them apart.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=99)** Their outcomes will all be the same.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=101)** This is an artifact of the way we use math to describe quantum states called global phase.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=107)** Mathematically a global phase is a complex number with a magnitude of one.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=112)** We can represent that in polar form as e raised to the power of i times theta, where theta is any real number.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=121)** When we multiply a quantum state vector by a global phase value, it's physically indistinguishable from the original state.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=128)** The global phase has no observable effect on the probabilities of each outcome when we measure that state.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=134)** We're mentioning this concept now because as we work through math examples throughout this course, we'll occasionally encounter global phase factors.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=141)** It's important to recognize that it's just a mathematical artifact and doesn't have any physical relevance to our quantum computations.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=147)** - We can usually ignore the global phase, however, there's another type of phase that does affect our quantum computations and that's the relative phase.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=156)** The relative phase is the difference between the phase vectors of our two probability amplitudes.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=161)** To see what that looks like in math, let's start by expressing the complex amplitudes alpha and beta using the polar notation.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=169)** We can write each complex value as a magnitude r times e to the power of i times a phase angle, theta.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=175)** Remember that in this representation the individual magnitudes and phase angles are real numbers, not complex.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=182)** Now, if we take that equation and use algebra to factor out the alpha phase factor, theta, that gives us an equivalent expression with that phase factor out in front.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=193)** You might recognize the format of this factor, e raise to the power of i times theta.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=198)** That's a complex value with a magnitude of one, which means it's a global phase factor.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=203)** It has no observable effect on our quantum states, so we can safely ignore it.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=207)** Mathematically, we can get rid of that global phase factor by multiplying this state by e to power of negative i times theta, which itself is also a global phase factor with no physical effect.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=219)** That cancels out the global phase we want to get rid of, leaving us with this expression.
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=224)** Now, to simplify this one more step.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=227)** Let's take that difference in theta values, which are both real numbers that do not appear anywhere else in this equation, and let's simplify it by rewriting it as a single variable, which will label as phi.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=239)** By ignoring the global phase and simplifying the difference in phase factors to a single variable, we've represented this quantum state using just three real numbers.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=249)** Having just three numbers means we can visualize this state as a point on a three-dimensional space like on the Bloch sphere.
>
> **[4:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=255)** Mapping this equation to the Bloch sphere requires some additional mathematical manipulation, but that's beyond the scope of this course.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=263)** - Now, let's talk about the remaining phase factor in this equation, phi, which is the difference between the phase factors of alpha and beta.
>
> **[4:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=271)** Phi represents the relative phase, which is something we can physically observe.
>
> **[4:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=276)** In regards to the Bloch sphere, this relative phase represents the azimuth angle around the Z axis.
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=282)** If we think of the Bloch sphere as a planet, a change in the relative phase corresponds to a change in longitude, rotating our quantum state around the pole along the same latitude.
>
> **[4:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=292)** - Rotating our quantum state around the Bloch sphere Z axis doesn't move us any closer to the zero or one state.
>
> **[4:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=299)** That means we're not changing the probabilities of measuring either of those states.
>
> **[5:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=303)** So how is that relative phase something we can physically observe?
>
> **[5:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=307)** - Great question, and you're right.
>
> **[5:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=309)** When we measure a qubit in the standard computational basis, the relative phase doesn't affect our probability of measuring one or zero, so it doesn't really matter.
>
> **[5:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=318)** However, there are other ways we can measure a qubit that do allow us to observe its relative phase, but that's an advanced topic we'll save for later in this course.
>
> **[5:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=328)** If your head is spinning from all this math talk of phase, don't worry.
>
> **[5:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=331)** Let's recap the three key concepts to take away from this lesson.
>
> **[5:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=335)** First, there's a thing called global phase, which can show up in our quantum equations.
>
> **[5:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=340)** This global phase is just part of the math we use to represent quantum states.
>
> **[5:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=344)** It's not something we can physically observe or measure, so we can usually ignore it.
>
> **[5:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=349)** The other type of phase that shows up in our quantum equations is the relative phase, which corresponds to an angle around the Z axis of the Bloch sphere.
>
> **[5:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=357)** The relative phase is something we can observe through certain types of measurements, so we do need to pay attention to it.
>
> **[6:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=364)** Finally, through some clever mathematical manipulations involving those two types of phase we can represent the observable elements of a quantum state using just three real numbers.
>
> **[6:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/global-and-relative-phase?u=76281980&t=374)** That's what enables us to visualize a quantum state in three dimensions as a point on the Bloch sphere.

> [!info]- Semantic Content

> **Definitions:** is an  (1), is a  (1)

>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/challenge-create-a-quantum-circuit?u=76281980&t=28)** Your program should simulate at least 1,000 shots of that circuit and then display the resulting counts on a histogram.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/challenge-create-a-quantum-circuit?u=76281980&t=35)** Since the qubit is initialized in the zero basis state and we're not applying any additional operations to change its state, that measurement outcome should always be zero.

#### Solution: Create a quantum circuit
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-create-a-quantum-circuit?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-create-a-quantum-circuit?u=76281980&t=0)** (bright music)
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-create-a-quantum-circuit?u=76281980&t=5)** - [Instructor] Let's look at our solution to the challenge of creating a quantum circuit.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-create-a-quantum-circuit?u=76281980&t=10)** Since we introduced all of the parts needed for this challenge in just the past few videos, rather than making you watch me type out all of that code again line by line, let's jump straight to the end result and talk through that.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-create-a-quantum-circuit?u=76281980&t=22)** The first notebook cell imports the Qiskit modules we'll need to use.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-create-a-quantum-circuit?u=76281980&t=26)** We imported all of the modules from the base Qiskit library with the command from Qiskit, import wildcard.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-create-a-quantum-circuit?u=76281980&t=33)** After that, we imported the visualization tools we'll need to use for this challenge, including plot_histogram and plot_bloch_multivector, and we also chose to use the array_to_latex function to help us display the state vector.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-create-a-quantum-circuit?u=76281980&t=46)** Finally, we included the command to use matplotlibs inline backend for displaying plots.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-create-a-quantum-circuit?u=76281980&t=52)** The next notebook cell builds the quantum circuit by instantiating a quantum register with one qubit and a classical register with one classical bit, combines those into a quantum circuit, and then calls the measure method on that circuit to measure the quantum register, qr, and place the result in the classical register, cr.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-create-a-quantum-circuit?u=76281980&t=72)** The third block of code simulates executing that circuit multiple times to plot a histogram of the outcomes.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-create-a-quantum-circuit?u=76281980&t=78)** For that, we needed to use the qasm_simulator, which we initialized by calling the Aer modules get_backend function.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-create-a-quantum-circuit?u=76281980&t=86)** We use the execute function to run the circuit using the simulator we just initialized as the backend and we simulate it for 1,024 shots.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-create-a-quantum-circuit?u=76281980&t=95)** Then, we get the resulting count value to plot a histogram, and as expected, the outcome is always zero because we didn't do anything to change the qubit state after initializing it.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-create-a-quantum-circuit?u=76281980&t=106)** Now, the last part of the challenge was to print the qubit state vector and display it on the Bloch sphere.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-create-a-quantum-circuit?u=76281980&t=112)** To do those things, we'll need to use a different simulator backend than the qasm_simulator we just used to get the counts for our histogram.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-create-a-quantum-circuit?u=76281980&t=120)** If there was a trick to this challenge, it was realizing that you'll need two different simulators.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-create-a-quantum-circuit?u=76281980&t=126)** The notebook cell after the histogram instantiates the state vector simulator backend and then uses that with the execute method to get the resulting state vector.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-create-a-quantum-circuit?u=76281980&t=135)** We used the array_to_latex function to print that state vector before the final cell uses the plot_bloch_multivector function to display it on the Bloch sphere.

### 2. Multiple Quantum Bits

#### Represent multiple qubits
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-multiple-qubits?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-multiple-qubits?u=76281980&t=0)** - So far, we've learned how to describe the state of a single qubit, which can exist in a superposition of our computational basis states: 0 and 1.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-multiple-qubits?u=76281980&t=9)** It may be interesting, but a quantum computer with only 1-qubit is not very useful.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-multiple-qubits?u=76281980&t=14)** To build a useful quantum program, we'll need to use multiple qubits.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-multiple-qubits?u=76281980&t=19)** - If we expand our quantum system to 2-qubits, now there are four possible basis states.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-multiple-qubits?u=76281980&t=24)** Both of our qubits could be 0, they could both be 1, or they could be different, and there are two ways that could happen.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-multiple-qubits?u=76281980&t=33)** Those four states are shown here using Dirac notation.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-multiple-qubits?u=76281980&t=37)** Although we'll verbally describe these states by reading the digits from left to right, keep in mind that they're written with the first bit- or least significant bit- in the rightmost position.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-multiple-qubits?u=76281980&t=48)** So, for example, in the state we'll refer to as 01, 1 is in the first position.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-multiple-qubits?u=76281980&t=54)** Now, to represent these four states in a vector space, we'll need four dimensions.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-multiple-qubits?u=76281980&t=59)** |00⟩ can be written as a four dimensional vector with a 1 in the first position and zeros elsewhere.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-multiple-qubits?u=76281980&t=66)** In the next state, |01⟩, that 1 is shifted down into the second position.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-multiple-qubits?u=76281980&t=72)** |10⟩ has it in the third position.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-multiple-qubits?u=76281980&t=76)** And the |11⟩ state vector has the 1 in the last position.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-multiple-qubits?u=76281980&t=80)** These are the four computational basis states for a 2-qubit system written in both direct notation and the corresponding column vectors.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-multiple-qubits?u=76281980&t=88)** - If we grow our system from 2-qubits to 3, that gives us eight basis states shown here.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-multiple-qubits?u=76281980&t=94)** Having eight states means we need eight dimensions to represent our set of orthonormal basis vectors.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-multiple-qubits?u=76281980&t=101)** We won't go through all of those vectors here but as an example, the state represented by |011⟩ would be an eight dimensional vector with a 1 in the fourth position and zeros everywhere else.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-multiple-qubits?u=76281980&t=113)** Expanding our quantum system again from 3 to 4-qubits grows our number of basis vectors from 8 to 16.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-multiple-qubits?u=76281980&t=120)** Representing a 5-qubit system requires 32 basis states and so on.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-multiple-qubits?u=76281980&t=125)** You may be recognizing a pattern here.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-multiple-qubits?u=76281980&t=127)** The number of qubits in the system means it will have two to that number of states.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-multiple-qubits?u=76281980&t=132)** So, for example, in a system with nine qubits, each individual qubit can be in one of two possible basis states, which is two to the power of nine or 512 states.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-multiple-qubits?u=76281980&t=144)** Those represent all the possible outcomes we could get when measuring those 9-qubits.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-multiple-qubits?u=76281980&t=149)** - If you've worked with classical computers in the past, this should seem familiar.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-multiple-qubits?u=76281980&t=153)** Because at the end of it all, when we measure quantum bits, we get back classical bits of information, ones and zeros.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-multiple-qubits?u=76281980&t=160)** But when we have a system with multiple qubits in superposition, they represent a linear combination of those however many basis states.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-multiple-qubits?u=76281980&t=169)** For example, to mathematically represent a superposition of 2-qubits, which have four basis states, we need four complex coefficients.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-multiple-qubits?u=76281980&t=179)** Instead of adding more Greek letters to the mix, we've simply used the Latin alphabet here labeling these variables as a, b, c, and d.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-multiple-qubits?u=76281980&t=187)** As an example, consider the quantum state where each of those variables is 1/2.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-multiple-qubits?u=76281980&t=192)** Represented as a vector, that would look something like this.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-multiple-qubits?u=76281980&t=196)** If we calculate the probability of measuring those 2-qubits and getting an outcome of 0 for both of them, we see that the probability of that is 1/4.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-multiple-qubits?u=76281980&t=206)** Similarly, the probability of measuring the first bit as 1 and the second bit as 0, is 1/4.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-multiple-qubits?u=76281980&t=212)** The opposite of that outcome has a probability of 1/4, as does the probability of measuring both qubits as being in the 1 state.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-multiple-qubits?u=76281980&t=221)** That example represents what's called a uniform superposition, where every possible outcome has an equal probability when you measure that set of qubits.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-multiple-qubits?u=76281980&t=230)** It's like flipping a pair of coins.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-multiple-qubits?u=76281980&t=233)** (coins clanging)
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-multiple-qubits?u=76281980&t=237)** - Now, let's look at another system of 2-qubits in a non-uniform superposition.
>
> **[4:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-multiple-qubits?u=76281980&t=242)** We've used a slightly different notation here to show that this superposition, |ψ⟩, is the sum of the basis states 00 and 01 both divided by the square root of two.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-multiple-qubits?u=76281980&t=253)** In vector notation, that corresponds to the first two positions being 1 over the square root of 2 and the last two positions being 0.
>
> **[4:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-multiple-qubits?u=76281980&t=262)** The probability of measuring this system of qubits and seeing both the zeros, is 1/2.
>
> **[4:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-multiple-qubits?u=76281980&t=267)** As is the probability of measuring the first qubit as 1 and the second qubit as 0.
>
> **[4:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-multiple-qubits?u=76281980&t=272)** The probability of observing these 2-qubits in any other combination, is 0.
>
> **[4:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-multiple-qubits?u=76281980&t=278)** So in this particular superposition of 2-qubits, we will always measure the second- or left qubit- as 0 and the outcome of measuring the first or rightmost qubit, will have a 50/50 chance of being 0 or 1.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Speakers:** - if (3), - so (1), - now (1)
> **Analogies:** for example (3), it's like (1)
> **Code Keywords:** else. (1), this. (1), let (1)
> **Cross-References:** in the next (1), in the last (1)
> **Warnings:** keep in mind (1)

#### How much information is in a qubit?
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/how-much-information-is-in-a-qubit?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/how-much-information-is-in-a-qubit?u=76281980&t=0)** - How much more information can a quantum computer store and process than a classical computer?
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/how-much-information-is-in-a-qubit?u=76281980&t=6)** News articles hyping up the future of quantum computing often talk about that but the way it's usually presented can be a bit misleading.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/how-much-information-is-in-a-qubit?u=76281980&t=13)** So let's take a moment to think through that question.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/how-much-information-is-in-a-qubit?u=76281980&t=16)** If we have a register of eight classical bits each of those bits can be in one of two possible states, one or zero, which means there are two to the power of eight or 256 possible combinations of bits.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/how-much-information-is-in-a-qubit?u=76281980&t=30)** To describe the state of that eight-bit classical register, at any given moment, we only need a single integer value which is usually a number between zero and 255.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/how-much-information-is-in-a-qubit?u=76281980&t=41)** That's how much information those eight classical bits can store.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/how-much-information-is-in-a-qubit?u=76281980&t=44)** - Now, let's think about how much information we need to describe the state of eight quantum bits.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/how-much-information-is-in-a-qubit?u=76281980&t=50)** Eight qubits will have two to the power of eight or 256 possible basis states.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/how-much-information-is-in-a-qubit?u=76281980&t=55)** However, unlike the classical bits those qubits can exist in a superposition consisting of all of those states at the same time.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/how-much-information-is-in-a-qubit?u=76281980&t=63)** That means we'll need 256 complex coefficients to describe the probability amplitudes for each of those basis states.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/how-much-information-is-in-a-qubit?u=76281980&t=71)** 256 complex numbers represent a lot more information than the single integer needed to specify the state of eight classical bits.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/how-much-information-is-in-a-qubit?u=76281980&t=80)** - That's certainly an impressive difference and it's an angle news stories like to hype up around quantum computers but it doesn't tell the whole story.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/how-much-information-is-in-a-qubit?u=76281980&t=87)** While qubits can certainly represent a lot of information while they're in superposition, when we measure those qubits they collapse to one of two possible states.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/how-much-information-is-in-a-qubit?u=76281980&t=96)** So the amount of information we can extract from our eight quantum bits is the same as what we get from eight classical bits and that's an important distinction to keep in mind as we write quantum programs.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/how-much-information-is-in-a-qubit?u=76281980&t=107)** We can leverage the unique properties of qubits to process information across enormous numbers of quantum states, but at the end of the day the output we get from a quantum algorithm is limited to classical information.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Speakers:** - how (1), - now (1), - that (1)
> **Code Keywords:** let (2)
> **Warnings:** keep in mind (1)


### 3. Single-Qubit Pauli Gates

#### Overview of matrix operations
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-matrix-operations?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-matrix-operations?u=76281980&t=0)** - The next section of this course will require us to multiply vectors and matrices.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-matrix-operations?u=76281980&t=5)** So let's have a quick refresher on how matrix multiplication works.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-matrix-operations?u=76281980&t=9)** Like the previous math refresher videos on vectors and complex numbers, if you're already comfortable multiplying vectors and matrices and finding the conjugate transpose of a matrix, feel free to skip ahead.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-matrix-operations?u=76281980&t=22)** A matrix is a rectangular array or table of values, usually written in brackets as shown here.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-matrix-operations?u=76281980&t=28)** We describe the dimensions of a matrix as the number of rows it has by the number of columns, often referred to by the variables m for the number of rows and n for the number of columns.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-matrix-operations?u=76281980&t=39)** For example, the matrix shown here has two rows and three columns, so it's a two-by-three matrix.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-matrix-operations?u=76281980&t=47)** This other example matrix has dimensions two-by-two and this third example is a three-by-one matrix.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-matrix-operations?u=76281980&t=54)** Notice that the three-by-one matrix is similar to a column vector with three elements.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-matrix-operations?u=76281980&t=60)** We can multiply two matrices together to calculate what's known as their dot product if the number of columns in the left matrix is equal to the number of rows in the right matrix, so the two-by-two matrix shown here can be multiplied by the two-by-one matrix because their inner dimensions match.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-matrix-operations?u=76281980&t=78)** However, if we swapped the order of those two matrices, we would not be able to multiply them together because their inner dimensions are now different.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-matrix-operations?u=76281980&t=87)** The order of the matrices matters for matrix multiplication, so let's swap them back.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-matrix-operations?u=76281980&t=94)** Now, the dimensions of the resulting matrix will be the number of rows in the left matrix by the number of columns in the right matrix.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-matrix-operations?u=76281980&t=102)** So multiplying these two matrices together will produce a two-by-one matrix.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-matrix-operations?u=76281980&t=108)** The first element of that matrix is calculated by taking the first rows of the left matrix which is a row vector ab and the first column of the right matrix.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-matrix-operations?u=76281980&t=118)** In this case, there's only one column, so that's the column vector xy.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-matrix-operations?u=76281980&t=123)** We then calculate the inner product of those two vectors by multiplying the corresponding elements and adding them all together as shown here.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-matrix-operations?u=76281980&t=130)** So the first element in the resulting matrix is equal to a times x plus b times y.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-matrix-operations?u=76281980&t=137)** The second element of the result matrix will be calculated in a similar manner using the second row in the left matrix and the only column in the right matrix, giving us the expression c times x plus d times y.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-matrix-operations?u=76281980&t=150)** Those are the basics of how to multiply two matrices together, which is a common operation we'll be using throughout the rest of this course.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-matrix-operations?u=76281980&t=157)** Now, while we're on the topic of matrices, there is a special matrix we should be familiar with and that's the identity matrix.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-matrix-operations?u=76281980&t=165)** The identity matrix is a square n by n matrix that has ones on its main diagonal and zeros everywhere else.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-matrix-operations?u=76281980&t=172)** For example, the matrix shown here is a two-by-two identity matrix.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-matrix-operations?u=76281980&t=177)** A three-by-three identity matrix follows the same diagonal pattern as does a four-by-four identity matrix and so on.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-matrix-operations?u=76281980&t=185)** We can have an identity matrix with any square dimensions.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-matrix-operations?u=76281980&t=189)** The thing that makes the identity matrix special is the property that if you multiply a vector or matrix by inappropriately-sized identity matrix, the result is the same.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-matrix-operations?u=76281980&t=199)** The identity doesn't change anything.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-matrix-operations?u=76281980&t=202)** As we dive into the math behind quantum operations, the identity matrix will show up from time to time, so it's good to be able to recognize it and understand its unique property.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-matrix-operations?u=76281980&t=211)** Now, there's one more matrix operation we'll need and that's the conjugate transpose.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-matrix-operations?u=76281980&t=216)** Finding the conjugate transpose of a matrix is similar to the conjugate transpose we've seen previously with vectors.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-matrix-operations?u=76281980&t=223)** It's a two-step process that involves transposing the matrix and applying the complex conjugate operation to each of its elements.
>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-matrix-operations?u=76281980&t=231)** We'll use the superscript dagger symbol to denote the conjugate transpose.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-matrix-operations?u=76281980&t=235)** The order in which we apply the transpose and conjugate operations doesn't matter.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-matrix-operations?u=76281980&t=239)** We could conjugate all of the elements first and then transpose the vector or vice versa.
>
> **[4:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-matrix-operations?u=76281980&t=244)** The result will be the same.
>
> **[4:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-matrix-operations?u=76281980&t=246)** For example, consider the matrix A shown here.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-matrix-operations?u=76281980&t=249)** We transpose a matrix by switching its rows with its columns which effectively flips the matrix elements over its diagonal.
>
> **[4:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-matrix-operations?u=76281980&t=257)** Then applying the complex conjugate to each of those individual elements gives us the conjugate transpose matrix A dagger.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/overview-of-matrix-operations?u=76281980&t=265)** Multiplication and the conjugate transpose are two essential matrix operations that we'll be using throughout the rest of this course.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Definitions:** is a  (7), known as (1)
> **Code Keywords:** let (2), require (1), match. (1), case, (1), else. (1)
> **Analogies:** for example (3)
> **Speakers:** - the (1)

#### Quantum logic gates
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-logic-gates?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-logic-gates?u=76281980&t=0)** - Now that we've seen how to represent information with qubits, we're ready to start writing algorithms to manipulate that data.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-logic-gates?u=76281980&t=7)** A quantum algorithm applies a specific sequence of operations to our qubits to change their quantum state in a controlled manner.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-logic-gates?u=76281980&t=14)** And a common way to visually represent those algorithms is what's called the quantum circuit model which is a diagram that shows the sequence of steps in a quantum computation.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-logic-gates?u=76281980&t=24)** Reading a quantum circuit diagram is similar to reading a sheet of music.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-logic-gates?u=76281980&t=28)** Each of the horizontal wires correspond to an individual qubit.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-logic-gates?u=76281980&t=32)** So the algorithm shown here uses three qubits which will all be initialized to the zero basis state at the beginning of this diagram on the far left.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-logic-gates?u=76281980&t=40)** This circuit also has three classical bits which we'll use to store the results when measuring those qubits.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-logic-gates?u=76281980&t=46)** Since we don't use those classical bits for anything other than storing results, we're combining them into a single composite line to save space on the diagram.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-logic-gates?u=76281980&t=55)** As with a sheet of music, time progresses going from left to right the various boxes and symbols on the diagram correspond to different quantum gates or instructions which tell the quantum computer to perform certain actions on one or more qubits.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-logic-gates?u=76281980&t=70)** Finally, at the end of our circuit we use the measurement instruction to read out the values of our three qubits and store the results in our classical registers.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-logic-gates?u=76281980&t=78)** In a real world physical quantum computer, executing each of those quantum gates might mean generating a specific electrical signal that affects the particles being used as cubits in the desired way.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-logic-gates?u=76281980&t=89)** - However, our intent for this course is to abstract away the physical implementation of quantum computers as much as possible.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-logic-gates?u=76281980&t=97)** To think about quantum operations at a higher level, and one way to do that is mathematically.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-logic-gates?u=76281980&t=102)** We can represent each of those quantum gates or operations as a matrix that we multiply with the state vector representing one or more qubits to perform a linear transformation on its value.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-logic-gates?u=76281980&t=114)** A gate that operates on just a single qubit can be represented as a two by two matrix.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-logic-gates?u=76281980&t=119)** When we multiply that by the state vector for a single qubit, the result will be a two by one matrix or vector representing the state of that qubit after the operation.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-logic-gates?u=76281980&t=129)** A quantum gate that operates on two qubits would be represented as a four by four matrix which we can multiply with the four dimensional vector representing the quantum state of those two qubits to get the resulting state of that system.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-logic-gates?u=76281980&t=142)** Later in this course, we'll even see one quantum gate that operates on three cubits at a time and is therefore represented as an eight by eight matrix.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-logic-gates?u=76281980&t=150)** - One important characteristic of these quantum logic gates is that the matrices we use to describe them are unitary.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-logic-gates?u=76281980&t=157)** A unitary matrix is a square matrix such that if we multiply it by its conjugate transposed we get the identity matrix I.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-logic-gates?u=76281980&t=165)** That's significant to quantum computing because a unitarian matrix will preserve the magnitude or norm when we multiply it against a state vector.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-logic-gates?u=76281980&t=174)** If we multiply a unitarian matrix operator with an arbitrary state vector Kets, the magnitude of the resulting state vector will be the same as the magnitude of the original state vector Kets.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-logic-gates?u=76281980&t=186)** Preserving that magnitude is essential because the quantum state vector should always have a magnitude of one, which is the sum of the probabilities for every possible outcome.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-logic-gates?u=76281980&t=195)** - Another important characteristic of quantum logic gates is that they're reversible.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-logic-gates?u=76281980&t=200)** That means if I give you the state of a qubit after applying a known quantum gate, you can apply an inverse operation to that qubit to determine what its quantum state was before it went through that gate effectively undoing or reversing the operation.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-logic-gates?u=76281980&t=215)** That's an important distinction between how quantum logic gates work and classical logic gates.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-logic-gates?u=76281980&t=220)** If you've worked with digital logic in the past, you may be familiar with classical logic gates like and, and or.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-logic-gates?u=76281980&t=227)** For example, an and gate takes two classical bits as inputs.
>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-logic-gates?u=76281980&t=231)** If both of those bits are in the one state, then it's output will also be one.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-logic-gates?u=76281980&t=236)** Otherwise, if any one of those two inputs are zero, then the output will be zero.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-logic-gates?u=76281980&t=241)** This classical logic gate is not reversible because if I were to tell you that the output of an AND gate is zero it would be impossible to determine what the two input bits were that produce that result.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-logic-gates?u=76281980&t=253)** As we'll see in the coming lessons.
>
> **[4:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-logic-gates?u=76281980&t=255)** Since quantum logic gates are reversible, that type of information is preserved in their output.
>
> **[4:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-logic-gates?u=76281980&t=261)** - Now, if the thought of matrix multiplication and complex math scares you, fair enough.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-logic-gates?u=76281980&t=265)** This is where our beach balls will come in handy.
>
> **[4:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-logic-gates?u=76281980&t=268)** Many of the quantum logic operations will learn about correspond to different types of rotations on the block sphere, moving the quantum state of a cubit from one point to another.
>
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-logic-gates?u=76281980&t=277)** - So if you've made your own block sphere model, grab that now and let's explore some quantum gates.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Speakers:** - now (2), - however (1), - one (1), - another (1), - so (1)
> **Code Keywords:** finally, (1), abstract (1), let (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (2)

#### Pauli-X gate
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-x-gate?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-x-gate?u=76281980&t=0)** - Three of the simplest yet most significant quantum logic operators are a set of related gates called the Pauli gates, which are named after the Nobel Prize-winning physicist, Wolfgang Pauli.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-x-gate?u=76281980&t=11)** The three Pauli gates each operate on a single qubit affecting its quantum state in different ways.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-x-gate?u=76281980&t=17)** Thinking in terms of the block sphere, each of the Pauli gates corresponds to a rotation of pi radians or 180 degrees around one of the three major axes.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-x-gate?u=76281980&t=27)** Mathematically, the Pauli gates are represented with the set of matrices shown here formally labeled as sigma X, sigma Y, and sigma Z.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-x-gate?u=76281980&t=36)** It's also common to see these gates simply labeled with the capital letters X, Y, and Z, which is how we'll be referring to them here.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-x-gate?u=76281980&t=44)** We'll explore these operations in the next few videos to understand their similarities and differences, starting with the X gate.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-x-gate?u=76281980&t=51)** When applied to a single qubit, the Pauli-X gate performs a bit flip, swapping the two basis states, zero and one.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-x-gate?u=76281980&t=58)** So if we use the X gate on a qubit in the zero state, the output will be one and vice versa.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-x-gate?u=76281980&t=65)** Applying this gate to one results in zero.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-x-gate?u=76281980&t=67)** This behavior makes the Pauli-X gate the quantum equivalent of the NOT gate used in classical computers, which inverts or flips the state of a classical bit between one and zero.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-x-gate?u=76281980&t=78)** For that reason, you'll sometimes see the Pauli-X gate simply referred to as the NOT gate.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-x-gate?u=76281980&t=84)** When drawing quantum circuit diagrams, we'll represent the Pauli-X gate as a block with a capital letter X in it.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-x-gate?u=76281980&t=90)** However, in some circumstances, you may also see it represented symbolically as a circle with crosshairs.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-x-gate?u=76281980&t=96)** We can simulate that quantum NOT operation with our block sphere beach ball by rotating it by pi radians or 180 degrees around the x-axis.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-x-gate?u=76281980&t=105)** For this demonstration, the point on the sphere facing you represents our current quantum state.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-x-gate?u=76281980&t=111)** So this qubit, as you see it, is currently in the zero state.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-x-gate?u=76281980&t=114)** To use the Pauli-X gate, I'll put a finger on each side of the x-axis and then rotate the sphere 180 degrees.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-x-gate?u=76281980&t=123)** That inverts our state, so now this qubit is in the one state.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-x-gate?u=76281980&t=127)** If I perform that same operation again a second time that rotates us back to where we started.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-x-gate?u=76281980&t=133)** So applying the X-gate twice in a row undoes its effect.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-x-gate?u=76281980&t=137)** The X-gate is its own inverse.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-x-gate?u=76281980&t=139)** - That makes sense for flipping between zero and one.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-x-gate?u=76281980&t=142)** But what about the other quantum states?
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-x-gate?u=76281980&t=144)** - Well, let's say our qubit is in a super position here, represented by this point on the sphere, which is halfway between zero and the positive x-axis.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-x-gate?u=76281980&t=154)** This point is closer to zero than one, so if we measure this quantum state it would have a higher probability of collapsing to zero than to one.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-x-gate?u=76281980&t=163)** Now to apply the Pauli-X gate with my fingers along the x-axis, I'll rotate that by 180 degrees and that transforms our qubit to this other state, halfway between one and the positive x-axis.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-x-gate?u=76281980&t=177)** We've flipped the bits and now our qubit has a higher probability of being measured as one than zero.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-x-gate?u=76281980&t=184)** - That's how we can think of the Pauli-X gate geometrically on the block sphere.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-x-gate?u=76281980&t=188)** Now, let's look at it mathematically.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-x-gate?u=76281980&t=190)** Let's apply the Pauli-X or NOT gate to an arbitrary quantum state with a complex amplitude alpha associated with the cat zero and beta associated with cat one.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-x-gate?u=76281980&t=200)** The result will be a state in which the alpha now applies to cat one and beta is a probability amplitude of cat zero.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-x-gate?u=76281980&t=207)** We've swapped or inverted the two basis states in this expression.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-x-gate?u=76281980&t=212)** As Baron mentioned earlier in this video, we can represent the Pauli-X gate as a two by two matrix shown here with ones in the top right and bottom left elements and zeros everywhere else.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-x-gate?u=76281980&t=223)** We can use this operator by multiplying it with a qubit state vector.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-x-gate?u=76281980&t=228)** In direct notation that's written as a capital X, representing the Pauli matrix to the left side of the cat.
>
> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-x-gate?u=76281980&t=234)** The order of operands matters and the Pauli matrix always goes on the left side of the cat or state vector.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-x-gate?u=76281980&t=241)** Now, if we multiply the Pauli-X matrix with the cat zero state vector, the first element of the result will be the dot product calculated at 0 X 1 + 1 X 0.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-x-gate?u=76281980&t=253)** And the second element will be 1 X 1 + 0 X 0.
>
> **[4:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-x-gate?u=76281980&t=257)** The result is a vector zero one, which corresponds to cat one.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-x-gate?u=76281980&t=263)** Similarly, if we apply the Pauli-X gate to the one state, as we should expect, the result is a zero state.
>
> **[4:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-x-gate?u=76281980&t=270)** And finally, when applying the Pauli-X gate to an arbitrary quantum state with coefficients alpha and beta that gives us a vector in which the locations of the alpha and beta have been swapped.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-x-gate?u=76281980&t=281)** Those are several different ways to think about the Pauli-X gate operation.
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-x-gate?u=76281980&t=286)** - Geometrically.
>
> **[4:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-x-gate?u=76281980&t=287)** - And mathematically.
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-x-gate?u=76281980&t=288)** Use whichever interpretation works best for you to understand those foundational concepts.

> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-x-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=256)** Now the block sphere is pointing downwards halfway between the one basis state and the positive X axis.
>
> **[4:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-x-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=262)** Using the Pauli-x gate, we flipped the probability amplitude so now there's a higher probability of measuring this qubit as one, than measuring it as zero.
