#### Pauli-Y gate
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=0)** - Our next quantum logic gate is the Pauli-Y gate.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=3)** And as its name suggests, it corresponds to a rotation of pie radiants or 180 degrees around the Y-axis of the Bloch sphere.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=12)** You'll see it represented on our quantum circuit diagrams as a box with a letter Y in the middle.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=17)** To use a Pauli-Y operator on this qubit, which is currently in the zero state, I'll put a finger on each side of the Y-axis and rotate it 80 degrees.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=27)** As you can see that rotation changed our quantum state from zero to one.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=32)** - Hang on a sec, that seems like what the Pauli-X gate did in a previous video.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=37)** In fact, if I take my qubit starting in the zero state and then put my fingers along its X-axis and rotated it 180 degrees, I also end up with a qubit in the one state.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=50)** So how are these two operations different?
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=52)** - Well, when applied to a qubit and one of our two standard basis states zero and one, which exists along the Z-axis, the Pauli-X and Pauli-Y gates will have a similar effect, flipping from one basis state to the other.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=65)** - But your one symbol is upside down whereas mine ended right side up because we rotated our spheres differently.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=72)** - Bingo, there's a bit more going on here.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=75)** We can see the difference between Pauli-X and Y more clearly if we use our two gates again on a super position state.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=82)** Let's begin the state halfway between zero and positive side of the X-axis.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=88)** - Okay, we're in the same starting state.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=91)** I'll put my fingers along the X-axis and rotate my sphere.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=95)** - And I'll do the same along the Y-axis.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=98)** - It looks like my resulting state is halfway between one and the positive side of the X-axis, but yours ended up between one and the negative X-axis.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=108)** So my quantum state stayed on the side of the sphere around the positive X-axis where the complex amplitudes are positive, while your quantum state flipped around to the opposite side of the sphere where the amplitudes are negative.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=121)** - Yep, measuring our two qubits would have the same outcome probabilities with a greater chance of collapsing to one than zero, but these are two distinct quantum states because they have different phases at different points of rotation around the Z-axis.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=136)** In addition to flipping between zero and one, using the Pauli-Y gate also flip the phase of my quantum state.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=143)** To see that another way, let's dig into the math behind it.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=146)** The Pauli-Y gate is most commonly represented as a two by two matrix shown here with negative I in the top right corner and positive I in the bottom left corner.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=156)** If we apply that gate to the cat zero state, we get an output vector whose first element is zero and the second element is I.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=164)** Because the first element to zero, we can easily factor out that I which shows us the result of using the Pauli-Y gate on cat zero is I times cat one.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=174)** So in addition to flipping the bit, the Pauli-Y gate also gave this state a phase factor of I.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=180)** If we calculate the measurement probabilities for that resulting state, we can see that the probability of measuring an outcome of zero is zero, and the probability of measuring a one is the absolute value of I squared, which equals one.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=194)** Those are the exact same outcome probabilities as the regular one state, which means there is no physically observable differences between cat one and I times cat one.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=204)** Then imaginary I on the front is just a global phase factor.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=208)** Next, let's see what happens when we use the Pauli-Y gate on the one basis state.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=213)** We can see the result that there is a vector with negative I as a first element and zero as a second element.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=219)** Again, since one of the elements is zero, we can easily factor out the negative I.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=225)** So the result of using the Pauli-Y gate on cat one is negative I times cat zero.
>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=231)** Calculating the outcome probabilities for that state gives us the same probability distribution as irregular zero state.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=238)** This qubit will always be measured as zero.
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=240)** So we see that applying the Pauli-Y operation on one of our two basis states flips the bit and applies a global phase.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=249)** - Now let's look at what happens when we use that gate on a qubit in super position.
>
> **[4:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=254)** If we use the Pauli-Y gate on an arbitrary quantum state with complex coefficients alpha and beta, the resulting vector has negative I times beta as the first element and I times alpha as the second element.
>
> **[4:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=267)** We can factor out the I from that vector, but we cannot factor out the negative vector from beta.
>
> **[4:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=273)** Written out using direct notation that resulting state vector looks like this.
>
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=277)** There are several things going on here so let's break it down piece by piece.
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=283)** This was our starting quantum state, cat I, and this is the result after using the Pauli-Y gate.
>
> **[4:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=289)** The first thing to notice is that we've flipped the bits by swapping the two basis states, zero and one.
>
> **[4:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=295)** Next is the I that we factored out which represents a global phase factor.
>
> **[5:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=300)** I is equal to E to the power of I times pi over two.
>
> **[5:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=304)** So the I on the front of this expression corresponds to a global phase factor of PI over two.
>
> **[5:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=311)** Finally, we have the factor of negative one which is equivalent to E to the power of I times pi.
>
> **[5:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=317)** That corresponds to a relative phase change of pi radiance or 180 degrees.
>
> **[5:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=324)** So in addition to flipping this bit which swaps our quantum state between the top half and bottom half of the block sphere, the Pauli-Y gate also flips its relative phase which moves the state between the front side and the backside of the Bloch sphere.
>
> **[5:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=338)** - Now, you may have noticed in each of those examples the Pauli-Y gate added on a global phase factor of I.
>
> **[5:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=344)** This is because Pauli-Y matrix has I's in the two non-zero positions.
>
> **[5:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=349)** - Can't we just ignore global phase though?
>
> **[5:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=352)** Because it doesn't physically affect anything.
>
> **[5:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=354)** It's just there as a mathematical artifact.
>
> **[5:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=356)** - And some people do.
>
> **[5:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=358)** In some textbooks, you may encounter this alternative version of the Pauli-Y matrix with all real values replacing the imaginary I's with ones.
>
> **[6:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=367)** That's a bit simpler and looks less intimidating without the I's.
>
> **[6:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=371)** However, there are some mathematical relationships between PauliX, Y, and Z matrices that break down without the imaginary values.
>
> **[6:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate?u=76281980&t=379)** That's a detail beyond the scope of this course, but to keep things proper, we'll be using the standard form of the Pauli-Y gate with the imaginary I's.

>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=5)** Our starting point is a basic circuit which is initialized in the second notebook cell to have a single qubit.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=11)** To use the Y gate on that qubit, we'll call the y function on that circuit, pass at the index of our one and only qubit, which is 0.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=22)** I'll click the Run All button to run the notebook.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=29)** That updates the quantum circuit diagram to include our Y gate, and on the Bloch sphere down below, our state has flipped from 0 to 1.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=37)** Looking at the statevector, which is printed just above the Bloch sphere, we can see that the second complex coefficient is now i, representing the imaginary unit.
>

> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=54)** If we change the Pauli-Y gate to a Pauli-X gate and rerun the notebook, the Bloch sphere visualization doesn't change, it's still pointing straight down towards the one state.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=69)** But now, the second element of the statevector is no longer imaginary, it's 1.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=75)** So, using the Pauli-X and Y gates on the zero basis state produces mathematically different results with differences in global phase, but there are physically indistinguishable from each other, and both represented by the same point on the bottom of the Bloch sphere whose measurement outcome will always be 1.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=93)** Now, to see how the Pauli-Y gate affects a more interesting superposition state, let's expand our quantum circuit to include two qubits and then use the ry gate to initialize both of those qubits into a superposition state.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=107)** Qiskit allows us to apply quantum gates to multiple bits using a single command by passing it a list of cubic indices enclosed in square brackets.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=119)** I'll run those cells, and we can see that both qubits are put into the same superposition located halfway between zero and the positive side of the Bloch sphere's x-axis.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=133)** Now let's add a Pauli-Y gate to our circuit applying it only to the second qubit.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=141)** I'll rerun those cells one last time, and we can see side by side how the Pauli-Y gate changes that superposition going from the state shown on the left Bloch sphere with qubit 0 to the right Bloch sphere with qubit 1.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-y-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=156)** Although it's tough to tell with the way these Bloch spheres are drawn, the state of qubit 1 is a point on the backside of the Bloch sphere halfway between one and the negative x-axis.

#### Pauli-Z gate
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-z-gate?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-z-gate?u=76281980&t=0)** - We've seen the Pauli-X and Pauli-Y gates.
>
> **[0:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-z-gate?u=76281980&t=2)** Which leaves us to talk about the third and final Pauli-Z gate.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-z-gate?u=76281980&t=6)** And as you can probably guess by now, the Pauli-Z gate corresponds to a rotation of Pi radians or 180 degrees around the Z axis of the block sphere.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-z-gate?u=76281980&t=15)** And you'll see it represented in our quantum circuit diagrams as a box with a letter Z in the middle.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-z-gate?u=76281980&t=21)** To apply the Pauli-Z operator to this qubit which is currently in the one basis state, I'll put a finger on each side of the Z axis and then rotate 180 degrees.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-z-gate?u=76281980&t=33)** - That didn't do anything.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-z-gate?u=76281980&t=35)** Your qubit is still one.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-z-gate?u=76281980&t=37)** - True, we started with one and ended with one.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-z-gate?u=76281980&t=40)** However, notice that now our label is upside down.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-z-gate?u=76281980&t=44)** - I see, so something happened when you applied the Pauli-Z gate, but one and upside down one are the exact same point on the block sphere.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-z-gate?u=76281980&t=54)** There's nothing to distinguish those two quantum states, they're really just the same thing.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-z-gate?u=76281980&t=59)** Let's try another example, may I?
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-z-gate?u=76281980&t=61)** - Sure.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-z-gate?u=76281980&t=64)** - Let's use the Pauli-Z gate on a qubit in this superposition state at the point on the sphere facing you.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-z-gate?u=76281980&t=69)** Halfway between zero and the positive side of the X-axis.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-z-gate?u=76281980&t=74)** I'll put my fingers along the Z-axis and then rotate the sphere 180 degrees.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-z-gate?u=76281980&t=80)** That puts us into a different superposition, halfway between zero and the negative side of the X-axis.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-z-gate?u=76281980&t=87)** - Unlike the Pauli X and Y gates, which flip the bit between a top half and bottom half of the block sphere.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-z-gate?u=76281980&t=93)** When Baron applied the Pauli-Z gate our quantum state remained on the top half of the block sphere.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-z-gate?u=76281980&t=98)** So it has the same probabilities of measuring one or zero as before.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-z-gate?u=76281980&t=102)** - However, the Pauli-Z gate did flip the phase of our quantum state, moving it from the front side of the block sphere to the backside.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-z-gate?u=76281980&t=110)** - Let's take a look at the math behind that.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-z-gate?u=76281980&t=113)** The Pauli-Z operator is represented as a two by two matrix shown here, with one in the top left corner and negative one in the bottom right.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-z-gate?u=76281980&t=121)** When we use the operator on the zero basis state, the result is zero.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-z-gate?u=76281980&t=126)** The Pauli-Z gate has absolutely no effect on it.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-z-gate?u=76281980&t=129)** However, when we apply it to KET1, that negative one from the Pauli-Z matrix gets picked up.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-z-gate?u=76281980&t=135)** Which gives us a result of negative KET1.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-z-gate?u=76281980&t=138)** That negative factor out front is a global phase factor and there isn't any distinguishable difference between negative KET1 and positive KET1.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-z-gate?u=76281980&t=146)** They both represent the same state on the bottom of the block sphere.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-z-gate?u=76281980&t=150)** - So using the Pauli-Z gate on either of the standard basis states doesn't really change anything.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-z-gate?u=76281980&t=156)** For the Poly Z gate to have any effect, our qubit has to be in a state that's somewhere not along the Z axis.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-z-gate?u=76281980&t=163)** If we use the Pauli-Z operator on the quantum state with complex coefficient Alpha and Beta the result is a state in which the Alpha coefficient is unchanged, but the sign of the Beta coefficient is negated.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-z-gate?u=76281980&t=175)** Which corresponds to flipping the qubit's relative phase by rotating it pi radians around the Z axis.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-z-gate?u=76281980&t=182)** Those are the basics of the Pauli-Z operator.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-z-gate?u=76281980&t=185)** And that completes our set of Pauli logic gates.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-z-gate?u=76281980&t=188)** - Before we go, let's review the effect of each Pauli operation when applied to a single qubit.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-z-gate?u=76281980&t=194)** Remember that the Pauli-X matrix will flip the bit, swapping the measurement probabilities between the two basis states.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-z-gate?u=76281980&t=201)** The Pauli-Y matrix flips the bit as well as the phase.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-z-gate?u=76281980&t=204)** And finally, the Pauli-Z matrix only flips the relative phase of the qubit, leaving its probability distribution unchanged.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Speakers:** - let (2), - we (1), - that (1), - true (1), - i (1)
> **Code Keywords:** let (4), finally, (1)
> **Env Vars:** ket1 (4)
> **Definitions:** is a  (2)

>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-z-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=7)** To demonstrate that, we'll start with a single qubit in its initial zero computational basis state.

> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-z-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=13)** Notice that the first element of our state vector has the real value one and that all numbers within that state vector are positive.

>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-z-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=48)** We went from the zero state to the zero state.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-z-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=51)** Nothing too exciting.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-z-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=52)** Now let's see what happens when we apply the Pauli-Z gate to a qubit in a state other than one of our computational basis states of zero or one.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-z-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=61)** To create a side-by-side demonstration, let's expand our circuit to have two qubits and then use the ry gate to put both of those qubits into a super position.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-z-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=79)** Then, after putting both qubits into the same super position, let's apply the Pauli-Z gate to the second qubit.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-z-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=87)** I'll change the index to do that.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-z-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=89)** Now I'll press the Run All button to run the notebook with those changes.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-z-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=95)** Now we can see what that superposition state looks like before applying a Z gate from the Bloch sphere on the left and then what it looks like after applying the Z gate from the Bloch sphere on the right.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/pauli-z-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=106)** Notice that the Z gate does not flip the probability so our quantum state vector stays on the top half of the Bloch sphere closer to zero, but it does flip the phase, which rotates our vector 180 degrees around the z-axis from the front side of the sphere to the back.

### 4. Single-Qubit Superposition Gates

#### Hadamard gate
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate?u=76281980&t=0)** - The three Pauli gates we've seen thus far are incredibly useful and you'll see them used throughout quantum programs.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate?u=76281980&t=7)** But if those are the only three gates we have to use it's impossible for us to take a qubit, which is initialized in the zero state, and put it into any state other than zero or one.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate?u=76281980&t=18)** That's not any different than working with classical bits.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate?u=76281980&t=22)** The Pauli gates are often used to initially set up or encode classical information into a few qubits at the beginning of a quantum program but to then transition our qubits into more interesting states of super position we're going to need a few more gates.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate?u=76281980&t=36)** - The most common gate for converting a qubit from one of the two basis states into a super position is the Hadamard gate named after the French mathematician Jacques Hadamard.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate?u=76281980&t=46)** Unlike the Pauli gates, which rotate around one of the major axes, x, y, and z, the Hadamard gate rotates our quantum state by pi radians or 180 degrees around the Bloch sphere vector [1 0 1] which is the vector pointing halfway between the x-axis and the z-axis.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate?u=76281980&t=65)** In our quantum circuit diagrams, you'll see the Hadamard gate represented as a box with the letter H in it.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate?u=76281980&t=71)** So what does that look like to rotate around that vector on the Bloch sphere?
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate?u=76281980&t=75)** - Let's use our Bloch sphere beach ball to visualize it.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate?u=76281980&t=78)** To apply a Hadamard gate, I'll put my finger on the point halfway between the zero state, which is at the top of the z-axis and the positive side of the x-axis.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate?u=76281980&t=89)** Then I'll place another finger on the opposite side of the ball.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate?u=76281980&t=93)** This will be our axis for rotation, which is at a 45 degree angle.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate?u=76281980&t=97)** Starting with our qubit in the zero state, I'll rotate it 180 degrees and that transitions us to the superposition state at the positive side of the x-axis.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate?u=76281980&t=108)** If I applied the Hadamard gate a second time on this quantum state, that rotates us another 180 degrees which brings us back to zero.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate?u=76281980&t=116)** So the Hadamard gate is its own inverse.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate?u=76281980&t=118)** Applying it twice in a row brings us back to where we started.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate?u=76281980&t=122)** - To apply the Hadamard gate to a qubit starting in the one state, Baron will put his fingers along the same vector as before then rotate the sphere 180 degrees, and that transitions us from one to the negative side of the x-axis.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate?u=76281980&t=139)** This is clearly a quantum gate because it takes our classical basis, states one and zero, and puts them into a superposition.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate?u=76281980&t=147)** - Let's look at that operation mathematically.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate?u=76281980&t=150)** The two by two matrix shown here represents the Hadamard gate.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate?u=76281980&t=154)** Notice that, unlike the three Pauli matrices we saw in previous videos, the Hadamard gate does not have any zero elements in it.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate?u=76281980&t=162)** Factoring out the scalar value of one over the square root of two makes it a little easier to see what's going on here.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate?u=76281980&t=168)** If we apply this Hadamard gate to a qubit in the zero basis state, that produces a matrix with one over the square root of two as both elements.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate?u=76281980&t=177)** We can write that using Dirac notation as the sum of ket zero and ket one over the square root of two.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate?u=76281980&t=183)** We'll refer to this state as ket plus, which is located at the positive end of the x-axis on the Bloch sphere.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate?u=76281980&t=190)** When we measure a qubit in this state, the probability of a zero outcome is the absolute value of one over the square root of two squared, which is one half.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate?u=76281980&t=200)** Along the same lines, the probability of measuring that qubit and getting an outcome of one is also one half.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate?u=76281980&t=207)** Measuring this quantum state in our standard basis is like flipping a coin between one and zero.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate?u=76281980&t=213)** - [Speaker 2] If we apply the same Hadamard gate to a qubit starting in the one basis state, the product is slightly different.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate?u=76281980&t=219)** Both elements of the state vector are one over the square root of two but now the bottom element is negative.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate?u=76281980&t=225)** That corresponds to a point on the Bloch sphere at the negative end of the x-axis which we'll represent in equations with the ket minus symbol.
>
> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate?u=76281980&t=234)** Measuring a qubit in this superposition state has a one half chance of resulting in a zero outcome and an equal probability that the outcome will be one.
>
> **[4:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate?u=76281980&t=244)** Applying the Hadamard gate to each of our two basis states results in one of two superpositioned states that look similar when measured.
>
> **[4:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate?u=76281980&t=251)** - However, they are distinct states with different phases that put them on opposite ends of the Bloch sphere's x-axis.
>
> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate?u=76281980&t=258)** The Hadamard gate is significant because it can transition our qubits from the classical realm of ones and zeros into the quantum realm of superposition which is where the magic really happens.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Speakers:** - the (2), - let (2), - to (1), - [speaker (1), - however (1)
> **Code Keywords:** super (2), let (2)
> **Prerequisites:** set up (1)

#### Hadamard gate with Qiskit
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980)

>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=0)** - Let's explore the Hadamard gate in action with qiskit.

>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=14)** However, as we can see from the circuit diagram it does not perform a measurement on that qubit.
>

>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=28)** After that, I add a measurement to the end of that circuit and redraw its diagram.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=33)** Then finally, I use the chasm simulator to simulate the circuit and plot a histogram of outcomes.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=39)** Since it's just a single qubit in the initial zero state we always get zero as the measured output.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=46)** Now let's add the Hadamard gate to that qubit by calling circuit.h and then passing in the index value zero.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=57)** I'll click the run all button to rerun this entire notebook and now we can examine that qubit after applying a Hadamard gate.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=66)** Notice that both probability amplitudes in the printed state vector are now the same.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=71)** They're both one over the square root of two.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=74)** Below that, we can see that on the block sphere the state vector is represented as an arrow pointing along the positive side of the x axis.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=83)** Finally, down at the bottom, we can see that measuring that qubit gave us roughly equal probabilities of each outcome.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=92)** Now let's insert a poly-X gate to flip the initial zero state to one before we apply the Hadamard operator.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=100)** I'll run the notebook again.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=106)** And now we see that the state vector on the block sphere points backwards towards the negative side of the X-axis.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=114)** Also, notice that the second element of the printed state vector just above that is now negative one over the square root of two.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=123)** However, if we look at the simulated measurements of that qubit, we see that it still has an equal chance of either outcome the same as before.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=131)** So from a measurement standpoint, this quantum state with the state vector pointing along the negative X-axis of the block sphere is indistinguishable from the previous state when the state vector pointed to the positive X axis.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=146)** At least we cannot tell those states apart when measuring in the standard basis which only gives us an outcome of zero or one.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=153)** Now for one last demonstration, let's apply the Hadamard gate a second time right after the first one remember that the Hadamard gate is its own inverse so applying it twice in a row will reverse the operation.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=170)** I'll run this notebook.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/hadamard-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=175)** And now we see that the resulting state is one because the two consecutive Hadamard gates have effectively canceled each other out.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** let (4), finally, (2)
> **File Paths:** circuit.h (1)
> **Speakers:** - let (1)

#### Measurement on an arbitrary basis
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/measurement-on-an-arbitrary-basis?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/measurement-on-an-arbitrary-basis?u=76281980&t=0)** - Now that we have the Hadamard gate and our bag of quantum tools, it's time to revisit the fundamental concept of measurement.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/measurement-on-an-arbitrary-basis?u=76281980&t=7)** So far in this course, we've always measured our qubits in the standard computational basis, composed of the states zero and one.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/measurement-on-an-arbitrary-basis?u=76281980&t=14)** Measuring a quantum state in that basis means we'll only observe either zero or one, each of which occurs with some probability.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/measurement-on-an-arbitrary-basis?u=76281980&t=21)** Since we represent those two states on opposite ends of the Bloch sphere's z-axis, this type of computational basis measurement is often called a Z measurement.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/measurement-on-an-arbitrary-basis?u=76281980&t=31)** But remember, from a mathematical standpoint, zero and one are not the only pair of basis states.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/measurement-on-an-arbitrary-basis?u=76281980&t=37)** Any two points on opposite sides of the Bloch sphere correspond to orthogonal state vectors which together form a valid pair of basis states.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/measurement-on-an-arbitrary-basis?u=76281980&t=45)** That means there's an infinite number of possible bases we can use to represent and measure a quantum state beyond just zero and one.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/measurement-on-an-arbitrary-basis?u=76281980&t=53)** - When we measure a quantum state, we can only extract a classical bit worth of information, giving us one of two outcomes.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/measurement-on-an-arbitrary-basis?u=76281980&t=60)** When we perform a standard z-axis measurement, it's like asking the question, is this quantum state ket one or zero?
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/measurement-on-an-arbitrary-basis?u=76281980&t=67)** Well, what if we want to ask a different question with our measurement?
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/measurement-on-an-arbitrary-basis?u=76281980&t=71)** Maybe we want to know if our qubit is in the ket plus or minus states which form a different basis pair along the x-axis.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/measurement-on-an-arbitrary-basis?u=76281980&t=78)** As we've seen in previous videos, performing a z-axis measurement on the plus state has an equal probability of measuring zero or one, and after that measurement, the qubit will no longer be in that previous superposition.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/measurement-on-an-arbitrary-basis?u=76281980&t=91)** That measurement doesn't help answer the question, was our qubit in the plus or minus state?
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/measurement-on-an-arbitrary-basis?u=76281980&t=97)** Because if it had been in the minus state, we would've had the same equal probability of those outcomes.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/measurement-on-an-arbitrary-basis?u=76281980&t=103)** To answer our question, we'll need to measure our qubit in a different basis.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/measurement-on-an-arbitrary-basis?u=76281980&t=107)** If we measure its state along the x-axis instead of the z-axis, now we're looking at whether it's in the plus or minus state, which together form an alternate basis.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/measurement-on-an-arbitrary-basis?u=76281980&t=117)** So if our qubit is in the minus state, then the outcome of the x-axis measurement, we'll always see it as minus.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/measurement-on-an-arbitrary-basis?u=76281980&t=124)** Likewise, if it's in the plus state, the x-axis measurement outcome will always be plus.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/measurement-on-an-arbitrary-basis?u=76281980&t=131)** However, if our qubit is in the zero state when we perform the x-axis measurement, we'll have an equal probability of getting either plus or minus as the outcome.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/measurement-on-an-arbitrary-basis?u=76281980&t=141)** Measuring a qubit in a different basis does not give us more information about its quantum state.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/measurement-on-an-arbitrary-basis?u=76281980&t=146)** It just gives us different information.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/measurement-on-an-arbitrary-basis?u=76281980&t=149)** - If we want to represent measuring in a different basis mathematically, we can do so easily by using Dirac notation.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/measurement-on-an-arbitrary-basis?u=76281980&t=155)** For example, the probability of measuring in arbitrary quantum state ket psi as the ket plus state is equal to the absolute value squared of the inner product bra plus times ket psi.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/measurement-on-an-arbitrary-basis?u=76281980&t=168)** If the qubit we were performing an x-axis measurement on was in the zero basis state, since our inner product of bra plus and ket zero is one over the square root of two, then the the probability of measuring it as ket plus is 1/2.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/measurement-on-an-arbitrary-basis?u=76281980&t=182)** The ket zero state has an equal probability of collapsing to ket plus or ket minus when measured in relation to the x-axis.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/measurement-on-an-arbitrary-basis?u=76281980&t=189)** If the qubit we were measuring was in the ket one state, then the probability of measuring it as ket plus would also be 1/2.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/measurement-on-an-arbitrary-basis?u=76281980&t=197)** However, if the state we were measuring was already ket plus, then the inner product of bra plus and ket plus is one which gives us 100% probability of measuring it as ket plus.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/measurement-on-an-arbitrary-basis?u=76281980&t=209)** Likewise, the probability of measuring the ket minus state as ket plus is zero because the inner product of bra plus and ket minus is zero.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/measurement-on-an-arbitrary-basis?u=76281980&t=218)** That's the concept of measuring in an arbitrary basis, but in practice, real quantum computers can usually only measure the difference between two physical states which serve as our computational basis along the z-axis.
>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/measurement-on-an-arbitrary-basis?u=76281980&t=231)** - Can we actually measure a real qubit in another basis?
>
> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/measurement-on-an-arbitrary-basis?u=76281980&t=234)** - Maybe not directly but we can simulate arbitrary measurements.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/measurement-on-an-arbitrary-basis?u=76281980&t=238)** By applying a rotational gate right before performing a Z measurement, we can manipulate our qubit to effectively choose which basis we're measuring it in.
>
> **[4:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/measurement-on-an-arbitrary-basis?u=76281980&t=247)** For example, to simulate a measurement along the x-axis we'll apply a Hadamard gate in front of our Z measurement.
>
> **[4:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/measurement-on-an-arbitrary-basis?u=76281980&t=254)** That rotates the qubit such that what was the x-axis is now effectively the z-axis.
>
> **[4:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/measurement-on-an-arbitrary-basis?u=76281980&t=260)** So if the qubit we intend to measure is in the plus state, applying that Hadamard gate will rotate it to the zero state and then the final Z measurement will always give us an outcome of zero.
>
> **[4:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/measurement-on-an-arbitrary-basis?u=76281980&t=271)** And from that information, we know that our qubit was in the plus state before we applied that Hadamard gate.
>
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/measurement-on-an-arbitrary-basis?u=76281980&t=277)** Along those lines, if it had originally been in the minus state, then applying the Hadamard gate would've rotated it to the one state which we would've then seen as the measurement outcome.
>
> **[4:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/measurement-on-an-arbitrary-basis?u=76281980&t=287)** Finally, notice that if we perform a simulated x-axis measurement on a qubit in one of the computational basis states, such as ket one, that process rotates the qubit into superposition along the x-axis, so we will no longer be able to tell what the qubit's original state was from the Z measurement result.
>
> **[5:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/measurement-on-an-arbitrary-basis?u=76281980&t=305)** This concept of measuring in arbitrary basis may seem like a quantum novelty at first glance.
>
> **[5:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/measurement-on-an-arbitrary-basis?u=76281980&t=311)** Sure, it's cool that we can do that, but why is it useful?
>
> **[5:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/measurement-on-an-arbitrary-basis?u=76281980&t=314)** - As we'll see near the end of this course, when we write a quantum algorithm, we encode and process information using various states of superposition.
>
> **[5:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/measurement-on-an-arbitrary-basis?u=76281980&t=323)** At the end of an algorithm, the result of that computation may be stored in a different basis.
>
> **[5:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/measurement-on-an-arbitrary-basis?u=76281980&t=328)** To extract an answer, we might want to know whether the qubit ended up in the plus or minus state.
>
> **[5:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/measurement-on-an-arbitrary-basis?u=76281980&t=334)** So to get that final information, we'll use additional logic gates to simulate measuring in the necessary basis.
>
> **[5:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/measurement-on-an-arbitrary-basis?u=76281980&t=341)** - In this video, we changed our measurement basis from the z-axis to the x-axis but there are countless other pairs of basis states we could use to measure a qubit.
>
> **[5:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/measurement-on-an-arbitrary-basis?u=76281980&t=350)** As we learn about more quantum logic gates for rotating a single qubit over the next few videos, think about how those gates could be used to simulate measuring in other possible bases.

#### Phase shift gates
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/phase-shift-gates?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/phase-shift-gates?u=76281980&t=0)** - So far, we've taken our qubits to one of four quantum states.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/phase-shift-gates?u=76281980&t=4)** Our two computational basis states, zero and one, and we've used the Hadamard gate to transform those into superposition states at both sides of the x-axis.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/phase-shift-gates?u=76281980&t=16)** But there's a lot more surface area on this Bloch sphere, so how do we move our qubits into some of those other possible superpositions?
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/phase-shift-gates?u=76281980&t=23)** - Well, one way to play with the quantum state is to apply a phase shift, rotating a qubit around the z-axis.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/phase-shift-gates?u=76281980&t=30)** We've already seen one type of phase shift gate, the Pauli Z gate, which rotates our qubit 180 degrees around the z-axis.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/phase-shift-gates?u=76281980&t=38)** That can transition our qubit from the positive x state to the negative x state.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/phase-shift-gates?u=76281980&t=44)** And if we apply it a second time, that brings us back to the positive x.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/phase-shift-gates?u=76281980&t=49)** - Both of those rotations passed over the y-axis so how can we transition to a state there?
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/phase-shift-gates?u=76281980&t=55)** - For that, we'll use a new gate called the S gate, which applies a phase shift of pi over two radians, rotating our qubit one quarter turn around the z-axis.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/phase-shift-gates?u=76281980&t=64)** So starting with the positive x state, the S gate will rotate our qubit a quarter turn to the positive y state.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/phase-shift-gates?u=76281980&t=71)** Looking at the math behind that, the S gate is represented by the matrix shown here with the elements 1 and i along the diagonal.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/phase-shift-gates?u=76281980&t=79)** You may also see it written using exponential notation to represent the imaginary number.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/phase-shift-gates?u=76281980&t=85)** These two matrices mean the same thing, just using different formats.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/phase-shift-gates?u=76281980&t=89)** If we apply the S gate to the ket plus state, which is the positive end of the Bloch sphere x-axis, that changes the second element of the state vector to i over square root of two.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/phase-shift-gates?u=76281980&t=101)** That puts us in the ket+ i state which is a positive end of the y-axis.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/phase-shift-gates?u=76281980&t=106)** - What happens if you apply the S gate again?
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/phase-shift-gates?u=76281980&t=109)** - Well, that's another core return in the same direction, which brings us around to negative X state.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/phase-shift-gates?u=76281980&t=116)** - So applying the S gate twice took us from positive X to positive Y, and then from positive Y to negative X.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/phase-shift-gates?u=76281980&t=126)** That means the S gate is not its own inverse.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/phase-shift-gates?u=76281980&t=128)** - You're catching on.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/phase-shift-gates?u=76281980&t=130)** Unlike all the gates we've previously seen, since the S gate only shifts the phase by pi over two radians, which is a 90 degree quarter turn, using it twice rotates the state by pi radians, which is a 180 half turn around the Bloch sphere.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/phase-shift-gates?u=76281980&t=145)** In fact, if we look at the product of multiplying the S gate matrix by itself, we can see that the resulting matrix is equivalent to the Poly Z gate, which is a 180 degree phase shift around the z-axis.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/phase-shift-gates?u=76281980&t=159)** - Seems simple enough.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/phase-shift-gates?u=76281980&t=160)** So if I want to shift the phase of a qubit by three pi over two radians, which is 270 degrees or three quarters turn, does that mean I should simply use that S gate three times in a row?
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/phase-shift-gates?u=76281980&t=172)** - Well, that will technically work, but it'd be more efficient to simply shift the phase by a quarter turn in the other direction.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/phase-shift-gates?u=76281980&t=180)** We can do that by using the S-dagger gate, which rotates a qubit by negative pi over two radians around the z-axis.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/phase-shift-gates?u=76281980&t=187)** It's represented by the matrix shown here, which is the conjugate transpose of the regular S gate matrix.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/phase-shift-gates?u=76281980&t=193)** Notice that the i in the bottom right element is now negative.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/phase-shift-gates?u=76281980&t=197)** You may also see it written like this using the exponential notation.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/phase-shift-gates?u=76281980&t=201)** If we multiply the regular S gate by the S-dagger gate, their product is the identity matrix, which does nothing.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/phase-shift-gates?u=76281980&t=208)** So when used back to back, these two gates should cancel each other out.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/phase-shift-gates?u=76281980&t=212)** - Seeing the relationship between the S and S-dagger gates reminds me of another important pair of phase shift gates, The T and T-dagger gates.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/phase-shift-gates?u=76281980&t=221)** Instead of rotating by pi over two radians around the z-axis like the S gates, the T gates shift the phase by pi over four radians around the z-axis.
>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/phase-shift-gates?u=76281980&t=231)** That means the T gate rotates our Bloch sphere beach ball by an eighth of a turn.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/phase-shift-gates?u=76281980&t=236)** - As you might expect, the T-dagger gate rotates the Bloch sphere by the same amount in the opposite direction.
>
> **[4:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/phase-shift-gates?u=76281980&t=242)** Looking at the matrix representations of those four S and T gates side by side makes it easy to see how they relate to each other.
>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/phase-shift-gates?u=76281980&t=250)** The S and S-dagger gates rotate by pi over two and negative pi over two radians around the z-axis respectively.
>
> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/phase-shift-gates?u=76281980&t=258)** And exponents in the T and T-dagger gate matrices show that they each shift the phase by pi over four radians in their respective directions.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Speakers:** - well (3), - so (2), - both (1), - for (1), - what (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (4)

#### Phase shift gates with Qiskit
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/phase-shift-gates-with-qiskit?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/phase-shift-gates-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Olivia] To demonstrate some of the phase shift gates with Qiskit, we'll use this simple circuit, which initializes three qubits with Hadamard gates.

> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/phase-shift-gates-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=17)** Now, to apply an S-gate to the middle qubit, I'll type circuit.s and give it the index of 1.

> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/phase-shift-gates-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=36)** I'll click the Run All button to run all the cells and we can see the updated circuit diagram and visualizations on the block spheres.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/phase-shift-gates-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=48)** Remember that the left qubit shows the state that all three qubits were in before we applied the S-gates.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/phase-shift-gates-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=55)** The S-gate phase shifted the middle qubit by pi over two radians, which rotated it 90 degrees counterclockwise around the Z-axis, and the S-dagger gate phase shifted the third qubit by negative pi over two radians, rotating it in the other direction, clockwise around the Z-axis by 90 degrees.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/phase-shift-gates-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=75)** Now, let's change those S and S-dagger gates into T and T-dagger gates.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/phase-shift-gates-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=82)** I'll press Shift + Enter twice to rerun all those cells and now those qubits are phase shifted in the same relative directions as before, but only by half as much.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/phase-shift-gates-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=95)** The T gate rotated the middle qubit counterclockwise by 45 degrees, or pi over four radians, and the T-dagger gate rotated the last cubit by negative pi over four radians in the other direction.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/phase-shift-gates-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=108)** This family of S and T gates is commonly used to phase shift cubits by these standard amounts.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** let (2)
> **Speakers:** - [olivia] (1)

#### Parameterized rotation gates
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/parameterized-rotation-gates?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/parameterized-rotation-gates?u=76281980&t=0)** - The family of S and T gates, the Hadamard gate and the Pauli X, Y, and Z gates, let us rotate qubits by common fixed amounts.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/parameterized-rotation-gates?u=76281980&t=8)** We can do a lot with those gates, but there's still a lot of Bloch sphere we haven't visited yet.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/parameterized-rotation-gates?u=76281980&t=13)** What if we want to put this qubit into a super position between the z and y-axes?
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/parameterized-rotation-gates?u=76281980&t=18)** - Easy; to get there, we can use a parameterized rotation operator.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/parameterized-rotation-gates?u=76281980&t=22)** These parameterized gates let us rotate our qubit state by an arbitrary number of degrees or radians around one of the three major axes.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/parameterized-rotation-gates?u=76281980&t=30)** They're symbolically represented in quantum circuit diagrams as Rx, Ry, and Rz blocks which indicate the number of radians for rotation.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/parameterized-rotation-gates?u=76281980&t=40)** - So with a qubit starting in the zero state, if we apply the Rx gate to rotate it by pi over four radians, which is 45 degrees, that rotates the qubit around the x-axis transitioning it to a state halfway between cat zero and the negative side of the y-axis.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/parameterized-rotation-gates?u=76281980&t=56)** - Exactly, and if instead of rotating it by positive pi over four, you rotate it by negative pi over four, that would spin the Bloch sphere in the opposite direction around the x-axis.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/parameterized-rotation-gates?u=76281980&t=66)** - Okay, that seems straightforward enough.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/parameterized-rotation-gates?u=76281980&t=69)** These parameterized gates let us apply an arbitrary amount of rotation around any of the three major axes.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/parameterized-rotation-gates?u=76281980&t=75)** What do these gates look like mathematically?
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/parameterized-rotation-gates?u=76281980&t=78)** - Brace yourself, because these are a bit more complicated than the gates we've seen thus far.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/parameterized-rotation-gates?u=76281980&t=82)** That's because they're generalized expressions to rotate the quantum state by any amount.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/parameterized-rotation-gates?u=76281980&t=87)** The Rotate x or Rx gate is represented by this two by two matrix of sine and cosine expressions and the Rotate y gate is a slight variation on that equation without the imaginary numbers.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/parameterized-rotation-gates?u=76281980&t=100)** In both expressions, the variable theta determines how many radians to rotate around the x or y-axis on the Bloch sphere.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/parameterized-rotation-gates?u=76281980&t=107)** Now, the Rz gate looks a little bit different represented by this two by two matrix with exponential expressions.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/parameterized-rotation-gates?u=76281980&t=115)** Instead of theta, we've used the variable five here to indicate that Rz gate applies the rotation around the z-axis which only affects the phase of a qubit.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/parameterized-rotation-gates?u=76281980&t=124)** - If we use one of these rotation gates, let's say Rx to rotate pi radians or 180 degrees around the x-axis, isn't that just the same as applying the Pauli X gate?
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/parameterized-rotation-gates?u=76281980&t=135)** - Kind of, but not quite.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/parameterized-rotation-gates?u=76281980&t=137)** In terms of how we visualize quantum states on the Bloch sphere, using the Rx gate to rotate pi radians flips our qubit in the same way as a Pauli X gate.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/parameterized-rotation-gates?u=76281980&t=146)** However, the math used to describe those two operations is slightly different.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/parameterized-rotation-gates?u=76281980&t=151)** If we take the Rx matrix and substitute pi for theta, those sines and cosines evaluate to this matrix containing zeros and negative i's.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/parameterized-rotation-gates?u=76281980&t=160)** - That reminds me of the matrix for the Pauli X gate, but slightly different.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/parameterized-rotation-gates?u=76281980&t=165)** - Exactly.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/parameterized-rotation-gates?u=76281980&t=166)** If we factor out that negative i, we do get the Pauli X matrix.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/parameterized-rotation-gates?u=76281980&t=170)** So using the Rx gate to rotate our qubit by pi radians is equal to negative i times the X gate.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/parameterized-rotation-gates?u=76281980&t=177)** If we walk through a similar process to evaluate the Ry gate for a rotation of pi radians, we see that it's equals i times the Y gate.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/parameterized-rotation-gates?u=76281980&t=185)** And along those lines, using the Rz gate to rotate by pi radians is equal to negative i times the Z gate.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/parameterized-rotation-gates?u=76281980&t=192)** These factors of i and negative i represent another example of a global phase.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/parameterized-rotation-gates?u=76281980&t=197)** They're just mathematical artifacts that don't affect the quantum state in an observable way.

> **Code Keywords:** let (4), super (1)
> **CLI Commands:** cat (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (1)
