#### Single-qubit gates on multi-qubit states
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/single-qubit-gates-on-multi-qubit-states?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/single-qubit-gates-on-multi-qubit-states?u=76281980&t=0)** - We've applied several different single cubic gates in isolation to one qubit at a time.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/single-qubit-gates-on-multi-qubit-states?u=76281980&t=6)** But what if we want to apply one of those gates to a qubit that's part of a multi-qubit system?
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/single-qubit-gates-on-multi-qubit-states?u=76281980&t=11)** For example, let's say we have a quantum circuit with two qubits.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/single-qubit-gates-on-multi-qubit-states?u=76281980&t=14)** They're both initialized in the zero basis state, and we want to apply an X gate to one of those qubits.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/single-qubit-gates-on-multi-qubit-states?u=76281980&t=20)** That operation is easy to understand by looking at the circuit diagram, but let's consider how to accomplish that mathematically.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/single-qubit-gates-on-multi-qubit-states?u=76281980&t=28)** The polyX operator is represented as a two-by-two matrix and the initial state vector for our pair of cubits is a four by one matrix.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/single-qubit-gates-on-multi-qubit-states?u=76281980&t=36)** We can't simply multiply the X-Matrix, and state vector like we've been doing in previous videos because the inner dimensions don't match.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/single-qubit-gates-on-multi-qubit-states?u=76281980&t=43)** These two matrices cannot be directly multiplied together.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/single-qubit-gates-on-multi-qubit-states?u=76281980&t=47)** Conceptually, it also makes sense that regular X gate can't be used here because there's nothing to indicate which of those two qubits is the one being flipped.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/single-qubit-gates-on-multi-qubit-states?u=76281980&t=56)** - We need to turn that two-by-two polyX matrix into a four-by-four matrix operator that we can apply to our multi-qubit state vector, and to do that, we'll need a new math tool.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/single-qubit-gates-on-multi-qubit-states?u=76281980&t=67)** Th Kronecker Product is an operation that takes two matrices of arbitrary sizes, and produces a block matrix as its output.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/single-qubit-gates-on-multi-qubit-states?u=76281980&t=75)** The term block matrix means a matrix that can be interpreted as a collection of blocks or subsections.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/single-qubit-gates-on-multi-qubit-states?u=76281980&t=80)** The Kronecker is a generalization of the common outer product operation, but instead of operating on two vectors, it works with matrices.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/single-qubit-gates-on-multi-qubit-states?u=76281980&t=89)** It's denoted by the same X in a circle symbol as the outer product.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/single-qubit-gates-on-multi-qubit-states?u=76281980&t=93)** Now, to understand how that operation works, let's calculate the Kronecker Product of these two matrices, which both have dimensions of two-by-two.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/single-qubit-gates-on-multi-qubit-states?u=76281980&t=102)** The first part of the resulting matrix, takes the top-left element of the left input matrix, and multiplies it against the entire right input matrix.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/single-qubit-gates-on-multi-qubit-states?u=76281980&t=111)** The next part is the top-right value of the left input matrix multiplied against once again, the entire right input matrix.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/single-qubit-gates-on-multi-qubit-states?u=76281980&t=119)** That pattern continues to produce the bottom left part, of the result and the bottom right part.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/single-qubit-gates-on-multi-qubit-states?u=76281980&t=125)** We can think of each of these four parts as subsections of the resulting block matrix.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/single-qubit-gates-on-multi-qubit-states?u=76281980&t=130)** The right input matrix is replicated in the four quadrants and scaled by the corresponding values of the left input matrix.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/single-qubit-gates-on-multi-qubit-states?u=76281980&t=137)** If we distribute those scaler values into those subsections, we end up with this four-by-four matrix.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/single-qubit-gates-on-multi-qubit-states?u=76281980&t=142)** Now, let's use that Kronecker Product to build the quantum operator we'll need for our circuit.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/single-qubit-gates-on-multi-qubit-states?u=76281980&t=148)** We only want to apply the X gate to the second qubit, but the Kronecker Product requires two inputs.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/single-qubit-gates-on-multi-qubit-states?u=76281980&t=154)** So to fill in that gap, we'll apply the identity gate to the first qubit.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/single-qubit-gates-on-multi-qubit-states?u=76281980&t=158)** This acts as a placeholder, which has no effect on the qubit state.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/single-qubit-gates-on-multi-qubit-states?u=76281980&t=162)** In the Kronecker Product, the gate operating on the second qubit is our left upper end, and the operation on the first qubit is the right upper end.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/single-qubit-gates-on-multi-qubit-states?u=76281980&t=171)** Looking at the resulting four-by-four matrix, notice that the identity matrix is replicated in the top right and bottom left corners, and all of of the other matrix elements are zero.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/single-qubit-gates-on-multi-qubit-states?u=76281980&t=181)** To simplify notation, you may see such a block matrix written like this with eyes to indicate identity matrices.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/single-qubit-gates-on-multi-qubit-states?u=76281980&t=188)** Keep in mind, even though it looks like a two-by-two matrix with that simplified notation, it's really still a four-by-four matrix under the hood.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/single-qubit-gates-on-multi-qubit-states?u=76281980&t=196)** Looking at that simplified form makes it easy to see that the identity matrices in the output are arranged similar to the ones in the polyX matrix.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/single-qubit-gates-on-multi-qubit-states?u=76281980&t=205)** Now, let's finally apply this new operator we just built to our two qubits both starting in the zero state.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/single-qubit-gates-on-multi-qubit-states?u=76281980&t=212)** Multiplying those together results in a four-by-one state vector with a one in the third position.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/single-qubit-gates-on-multi-qubit-states?u=76281980&t=218)** That corresponds to the one zero basis state, which means we've successfully applied the polyX gate to the second of our two qubit pair while leaving the first qubit alone.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/single-qubit-gates-on-multi-qubit-states?u=76281980&t=228)** This process, using the Kronecker Product is how we can build operators that apply single qubit gates to multi-qubit state vectors.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** let (5), match. (1)
> **Code Identifiers:** polyx (4)
> **Definitions:** is a  (2), is an  (1)
> **Speakers:** - we (2)
> **Analogies:** for example (1)
> **Warnings:** keep in mind (1)

#### Challenge: Random numbers
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/challenge-random-numbers?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/challenge-random-numbers?u=76281980&t=0)** (inquisitive music)
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/challenge-random-numbers?u=76281980&t=5)** - We've learned about several quantum gates that can put a single qubit into arbitrary states of superposition, so I think it's time for another challenge.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/challenge-random-numbers?u=76281980&t=14)** Your goal will be to take a new quantum circuit with three qubits and then use single qubit gates to configure their state such that when you measure those three qubits, they'll have roughly the outcome distribution shown here with a 37.5% probability of measuring those three qubits as 001 or 011, and a 12.5% chance of measuring them as 101 or 111.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/challenge-random-numbers?u=76281980&t=42)** This challenge is a bit of a puzzle.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/challenge-random-numbers?u=76281980&t=44)** You'll need to work backwards from those measurement results to figure out a set of quantum states that might have produced them.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/challenge-random-numbers?u=76281980&t=50)** Now, don't get too caught up in trying to match those probabilities exactly.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/challenge-random-numbers?u=76281980&t=55)** As long as your quantum circuit puts those three qubits into states with roughly the same distribution of those four outcomes, you've achieved the intent of this challenge.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/challenge-random-numbers?u=76281980&t=64)** To give you a starting point, we've included this simple framework which creates a new quantum circuit with three qubits in the second notebook cell.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/challenge-random-numbers?u=76281980&t=72)** You can replace the comment after that with your code to configure those qubits before the program calls the measure_all method on the circuit.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/challenge-random-numbers?u=76281980&t=80)** As its name suggests, the measure_all method adds a measurement operation to all qubits in the circuit, and then by default it creates a new register of classical bits to store the results.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/challenge-random-numbers?u=76281980&t=91)** There are technically an infinite number of potential solutions to this challenge, but for starters see if you can find at least one.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/challenge-random-numbers?u=76281980&t=99)** Take some time to solve the problem then watch the next video to see how we approached it.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/challenge-random-numbers?u=76281980&t=103)** Good luck.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Prerequisites:** configure (2), you'll need (1)
> **Code Identifiers:** measure_all (2)
> **Versions:** 37.5 (1), 12.5 (1)
> **CLI Commands:** find (1)
> **Cross-References:** next video (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (1)
> **Speakers:** - we (1)
> **Non-Speech:** (inquisitive music) (1)

#### Solution: Random numbers
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-random-numbers?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-random-numbers?u=76281980&t=0)** (bright music)
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-random-numbers?u=76281980&t=5)** - [Instructor] Let's talk through how we approach the challenge to put three qubits into quantum states with a specific outcome distribution.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-random-numbers?u=76281980&t=13)** Since each of the qubits are independent of each other, we can think about them one at a time.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-random-numbers?u=76281980&t=18)** First, looking at the four possible outcomes, notice that the first qubit at index zero is always measured as one, meaning that qubit needs to be completely flipped from its initial zero state.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-random-numbers?u=76281980&t=31)** So let's insert a Pauli-X gate into our quantum circuit to do that.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-random-numbers?u=76281980&t=36)** Next, looking at just the second qubit, we can see that 50% of the time, it has an outcome of zero, and 50% of the time, its outcome is one.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-random-numbers?u=76281980&t=47)** That means the second qubit needs to be at an equal superposition between these zero and one states and we can accomplish that by applying a Hadamard Gate to it.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-random-numbers?u=76281980&t=57)** Now, shifting focus to the third qubit, while looking across these four possible outcomes, if we add together the probabilities of measuring zero, they total 75% and there's a 25% chance of measuring that qubit as one.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-random-numbers?u=76281980&t=72)** That means we'll need to apply a rotation that puts it in a super position with a higher probability of measuring a zero outcome than one.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-random-numbers?u=76281980&t=81)** I chose to accomplish that by using a parameterized gate to rotate the third qubit by pi over three radians around the y-axis.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-random-numbers?u=76281980&t=90)** Here you can see my code to produce that circuit with the Pauli-X, Hadamard and Y-Rotation Gates applied to the corresponding qubits.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-random-numbers?u=76281980&t=99)** When we simulate that circuit for a million shots, we can see the four desired outcome probabilities on the histogram.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-random-numbers?u=76281980&t=106)** If you came up with a different set of gates to solve that challenge, great.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-random-numbers?u=76281980&t=109)** In theory, there are an infinite number of possible solutions.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-random-numbers?u=76281980&t=113)** So if you enjoyed this challenge, I encourage you to take a few minutes to think of other sets of gates that could be used to achieve the same outcomes.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** let (2), super (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)
> **Non-Speech:** (bright music) (1)


### 5. Multi-Qubit Gates

#### Controlled-NOT (CNOT) gate
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/controlled-not-cnot-gate?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/controlled-not-cnot-gate?u=76281980&t=0)** - Thus far, we've only explored the quantum state of one qubit at a time.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/controlled-not-cnot-gate?u=76281980&t=4)** - Sure, we've built circuits with multiple qubits, but those qubits have all been completely independent with the state of one qubit having no impact on any of the other qubits.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/controlled-not-cnot-gate?u=76281980&t=14)** - To build quantum programs that do more than just generate random numbers, we need to create interactions and dependencies between multiple qubits.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/controlled-not-cnot-gate?u=76281980&t=22)** - It's time to add another quantum gate to our bag of tools.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/controlled-not-cnot-gate?u=76281980&t=25)** The controlled NOT gate is a quantum operation involving two qubits, which conditionally flips the state of a target qubit by performing a Pauli-X operation if and only if the state of the other controlled qubit is one.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/controlled-not-cnot-gate?u=76281980&t=39)** You'll usually see this gate abbreviated as CNOT, though sometimes you may see it referred to as CX, which stands for controlled X gate.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/controlled-not-cnot-gate?u=76281980&t=48)** The CNOT gate is represented in circuit diagrams with the symbol shown here which connects the two qubits involved with a line between them.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/controlled-not-cnot-gate?u=76281980&t=55)** The smaller dot symbol indicates the control qubit, and the larger circle with a plus symbol indicates the target qubit.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/controlled-not-cnot-gate?u=76281980&t=63)** Let's demonstrate that CNOT operation using our Bloch sphere beach balls.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/controlled-not-cnot-gate?u=76281980&t=67)** - My qubit will be the control qubit.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/controlled-not-cnot-gate?u=76281980&t=69)** - I guess that means I'm the target.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/controlled-not-cnot-gate?u=76281980&t=71)** - We'll represent the CNOT gate with a piece of string between our qubits.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/controlled-not-cnot-gate?u=76281980&t=75)** - When we apply the gate, since Olivia's control is zero, I don't do anything to my target qubit.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/controlled-not-cnot-gate?u=76281980&t=82)** - But if I'm in the one state when we apply that gate...
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/controlled-not-cnot-gate?u=76281980&t=86)** - Then my qubit gets flipped.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/controlled-not-cnot-gate?u=76281980&t=89)** Notice that in both cases, Olivia's control qubit was left unchanged.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/controlled-not-cnot-gate?u=76281980&t=94)** Since that state information is preserved by the operation, the CNOT gate is easily reversible.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/controlled-not-cnot-gate?u=76281980&t=100)** - If we apply that gate another time...
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/controlled-not-cnot-gate?u=76281980&t=102)** - That flips my state again.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/controlled-not-cnot-gate?u=76281980&t=104)** - And now we're back to where we started.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/controlled-not-cnot-gate?u=76281980&t=107)** Mathematically, the CNOT gate can be represented as a four by four matrix seen here.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/controlled-not-cnot-gate?u=76281980&t=112)** Notice that the top left quadrant resembles an identity matrix with ones along the main diagonal, and the bottom right quadrant is similar to the Pauli-X matrix.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/controlled-not-cnot-gate?u=76281980&t=122)** Now, if we apply the CNOT gate to a pair of qubits in the zero-zero state, since the qubit in the control position is zero, the result is unchanged; it's zero-zero.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/controlled-not-cnot-gate?u=76281980&t=132)** Along the same lines, if we apply the CNOT gate to the zero-one state, since the control bit is still zero, the result will also be the same, zero-one.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/controlled-not-cnot-gate?u=76281980&t=141)** However, if we apply the CNOT to the one-zero state, since now the control bit is one, the target bit will be flipped on the output, resulting in the one-one state.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/controlled-not-cnot-gate?u=76281980&t=151)** And along the same lines, if we apply CNOT to the one-one state, the target qubit will be flipped in the result, leaving us with one-zero.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/controlled-not-cnot-gate?u=76281980&t=159)** - This CNOT gate is just the first of several quantum gates we'll learn about to operate on multiple qubits.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Speakers:** - thus (1), - sure (1), - to (1), - it (1), - my (1)
> **Env Vars:** cnot (11)
> **Definitions:** is a  (1), stands for (1)
> **Code Keywords:** let (1)

> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/controlled-not-cnot-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=59)** So, let's insert an X gate to flip that control to one just before the C-NOT.

>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/controlled-not-cnot-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=79)** The X gate flipped the first qubit, zero.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/controlled-not-cnot-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=81)** Then, because that control qubit was in the one state, the C-NOT flipped the other target qubit.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/controlled-not-cnot-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=88)** Now, the C-NOT gate will only flip the target qubit if the control qubit is one at the time the C-NOT is executed.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/controlled-not-cnot-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=95)** Let's extend this example to have three qubits.
>

> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/controlled-not-cnot-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=107)** Notice that this time, instead of using the cnot method, I've used the cx method for controlled-X gate.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/controlled-not-cnot-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=114)** This means the exact same thing in Qiskit, it's just a different way to write it, and depending on who writes the code, you might see either one.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/controlled-not-cnot-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=122)** We'll use the second qubit as the control for the C-NOT gate, and the third qubit as it's target.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/controlled-not-cnot-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=130)** When I run the simulation with that new circuit.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/controlled-not-cnot-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=135)** We see that our qubits are in the 1-1-1 state.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/controlled-not-cnot-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=139)** After the initial X gate flipped the first qubit, the first C-NOT flips the middle qubit from zero to one because it's top control qubit was one.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/controlled-not-cnot-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=148)** Then, because the middle qubit is now in the one state, the second C-NOT gate flips the last qubit as its target.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/controlled-not-cnot-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=156)** If I use copy and paste to swap around the order of those two C-NOT gates.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/controlled-not-cnot-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=166)** And then run those cells again.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/controlled-not-cnot-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=169)** Now, we get a different outcome.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/controlled-not-cnot-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=172)** In this circuit, the first C-NOT gate did not flip the last qubit as its target, because at the time it was executed, the middle control qubit was still zero.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/controlled-not-cnot-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=182)** However, the second C-NOT gate did flip its target in the middle because when it was executed, the first qubit was already flipped to one.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/controlled-not-cnot-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=191)** As we can see here, the order in which these quantum logic gates are applied matters, and putting them in a different order can produce different results.

#### Toffoli gate
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/toffoli-gate?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/toffoli-gate?u=76281980&t=0)** - Now that we've learned about the controlled NOT gate which conditionally flips the state of a target qubit based on a single control bit, let's extend that concept from two qubits to three qubits with the controlled controlled NOT gate, also known as the Toffoli gate.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/toffoli-gate?u=76281980&t=15)** The Toffoli gate uses two qubits as controls which are indicated on its circuit diagram with small dots.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/toffoli-gate?u=76281980&t=21)** If and only if both of those control qubits are 1 the Toffoli gate will apply a POLYX operation to flip the state of the third target qubit, which is indicated with the circle and plus symbol.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/toffoli-gate?u=76281980&t=33)** You'll often see this gate abbreviated as CCNOT or as CCX which stands for controlled controlled-X gate.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/toffoli-gate?u=76281980&t=42)** If these two beach balls that I'm holding represent the control qubits-
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/toffoli-gate?u=76281980&t=45)** - And I'm holding the target qubit-
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/toffoli-gate?u=76281980&t=47)** - Then if my qubits are in any state other than 1-1, that could be 0-0, 0-1, or 1-0-
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/toffoli-gate?u=76281980&t=55)** - Then I don't do anything.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/toffoli-gate?u=76281980&t=57)** - But if both control qubits are in the 1-1 state, when we apply the Toffoli gate-
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/toffoli-gate?u=76281980&t=62)** - My cubic gets flipped.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/toffoli-gate?u=76281980&t=64)** Mathematically, the Toffoli, or CCNOT gate, is represented by this eight-by-eight matrix.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/toffoli-gate?u=76281980&t=70)** Notice that the top left section resembles an identity matrix with 1s along the main diagonal.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/toffoli-gate?u=76281980&t=76)** If we have a set of qubits in which at least one of the controls is 0, that part of the matrix will pass through the state vector leaving the target unchanged.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/toffoli-gate?u=76281980&t=86)** For example, if we apply the Toffoli gate to three qubits that are all in the 0 state, that state vector passes through unchanged.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/toffoli-gate?u=76281980&t=94)** The same thing happens with the vector for the 0-0-1 state, 0-1-0, 0-1-1, 1-0-0, and 1-0-1.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/toffoli-gate?u=76281980&t=103)** However, when we apply the Toffoli gate to a three qubit state in which both control bits are 1, such as 1-1-0, now the bottom right corner of the matrix comes into play.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/toffoli-gate?u=76281980&t=114)** Flipping the target qubit from 0 to 1.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/toffoli-gate?u=76281980&t=117)** Similarly, when the Toffoli gate is applied to the 1-1-1 state, it flips the target from 1 to 0.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/toffoli-gate?u=76281980&t=123)** Notice that regardless of whether or not the target qubit is flipped, the two control qubits are always left unchanged.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/toffoli-gate?u=76281980&t=130)** That's similar to what we saw previously with the C-NOT gate.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/toffoli-gate?u=76281980&t=134)** Since that state information is preserved in the output, the Toffoli gate is easily reversible.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/toffoli-gate?u=76281980&t=139)** And applying it twice in a row undoes its effect because the CCNOT gate, is its own inverse.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Speakers:** - then (2), - now (1), - and (1), - but (1), - my (1)
> **Env Vars:** ccnot (3), polyx (1), ccx (1)
> **Analogies:** for example (1), such as (1), similar to (1)
> **Code Keywords:** let (1), pass (1)
> **Definitions:** known as (1), stands for (1)

> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/toffoli-gate-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=145)** That shows us that the toffoli gate rotated the target qubit 180 degrees around the X axis.

#### Swap and Fredkin gates
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/swap-and-fredkin-gates?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/swap-and-fredkin-gates?u=76281980&t=0)** - We've used the CNOT and Toffoli gates to conditionally flip the state of a target qubit, now let's learn about two more multi qubit gates which can be used to swap the state of two qubits.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/swap-and-fredkin-gates?u=76281980&t=11)** The first one is called, well, SWAP.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/swap-and-fredkin-gates?u=76281980&t=14)** As its name implies, the SWAP gate swaps the state of two qubits.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/swap-and-fredkin-gates?u=76281980&t=18)** It's represented on a circuit diagram as X's on the two qubits to swap and a line connecting them.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/swap-and-fredkin-gates?u=76281980&t=24)** Let's say Olivia and I use the SWAP gate on our two qubits.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/swap-and-fredkin-gates?u=76281980&t=28)** If our qubits are both in the same state.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/swap-and-fredkin-gates?u=76281980&t=31)** - Then swapping doesn't really do anything.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/swap-and-fredkin-gates?u=76281980&t=33)** But if my qubit starts in a different state, let's say I start in the one state.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/swap-and-fredkin-gates?u=76281980&t=37)** - And my qubit is zero.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/swap-and-fredkin-gates?u=76281980&t=39)** - Then if we apply the SWAP gate, then now my qubit is zero.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/swap-and-fredkin-gates?u=76281980&t=44)** - And I'm holding one.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/swap-and-fredkin-gates?u=76281980&t=45)** - Notice that Baron and I didn't physically trade beach balls, I'm still holding the same qubit as before.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/swap-and-fredkin-gates?u=76281980&t=50)** - And I've also got my original qubit.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/swap-and-fredkin-gates?u=76281980&t=53)** - What we swapped was the state of these two qubits.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/swap-and-fredkin-gates?u=76281980&t=56)** Looking at the matrix representation of the SWAP operation, if we apply it to a pair of qubits in the zero state, the one in the top left corner of the SWAP matrix preserves that state leaving the output unchanged.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/swap-and-fredkin-gates?u=76281980&t=68)** Similarly, trying to swap a pair of qubits are both one doesn't have any effect.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/swap-and-fredkin-gates?u=76281980&t=74)** However, when we apply SWAP to any other state in which there is a difference between the two qubits, the middle portion of the matrix comes into play, swapping the middle part of the state vector.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/swap-and-fredkin-gates?u=76281980&t=85)** - If we want to control whether or not these two qubits are swapped, we can use a close relative of the SWAP gate known as the Fredkin gate.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/swap-and-fredkin-gates?u=76281980&t=93)** The Fredkin, or Controlled-SWAP gate, conditionally swaps the state of two target qubits if and only if a controlled qubit is in the one state.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/swap-and-fredkin-gates?u=76281980&t=101)** On its circuit diagram symbol, shown here, the small dot indicates the control qubit and the two X's indicate the target qubits to conditionally swap.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/swap-and-fredkin-gates?u=76281980&t=111)** Mathematically, the Conditional-SWAP, or C-SWAP gate, is represented by the matrix shown here.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/swap-and-fredkin-gates?u=76281980&t=116)** Notice that top left quadrant resembles an identity matrix.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/swap-and-fredkin-gates?u=76281980&t=120)** If we use C-SWAP on a state vector in which the first control qubit is zero, then nothing happens.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/swap-and-fredkin-gates?u=76281980&t=127)** However, if we apply its C-SWAP to a three qubit system where the control qubit is one, then the bottom right quadrant will come into play, which resembles the SWAP gate and swaps the states of the target qubits in the output.

#### Challenge: Classical two-bit adder
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/challenge-classical-two-bit-adder?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/challenge-classical-two-bit-adder?u=76281980&t=0)** - Now that we've seen several new logic gates that work on multiple qubits, it's time for another challenge to practice using them.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/challenge-classical-two-bit-adder?u=76281980&t=13)** Your goal for this challenge is to build a circuit using quantum logic gates that performs the same operation as a two-bit binary adder.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/challenge-classical-two-bit-adder?u=76281980&t=21)** In classical digital logic, a two-bit adder can be implemented using an XOR and AND gate, as shown here, to add together the two input bits, A and B.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/challenge-classical-two-bit-adder?u=76281980&t=32)** The output from the XOR represents the sum of those two bits, and the output from the AND gate is an extra carry-out value.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/challenge-classical-two-bit-adder?u=76281980&t=40)** If you've never heard of AND and XOR gate before, don't worry.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/challenge-classical-two-bit-adder?u=76281980&t=44)** We can look at this logic table to see what the expected output should be for each of the four possible input states.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/challenge-classical-two-bit-adder?u=76281980&t=51)** If both input bits are zero, then the sum and carry-out bits should both be zero, because zero plus zero equals, well, zero.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/challenge-classical-two-bit-adder?u=76281980&t=59)** If only one of the input bits is one and the other bit is zero, then the sum bit should be one on the output, while the carry-out remains as zero.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/challenge-classical-two-bit-adder?u=76281980&t=68)** And if both input bits are one, since one plus one is two, to represent that with our binary output, we raise the carry-out bit to one and set the sum bit to zero.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/challenge-classical-two-bit-adder?u=76281980&t=80)** That's how a two-bit binary adder can be implemented using classical digital logic gates.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/challenge-classical-two-bit-adder?u=76281980&t=85)** For this challenge, we'll be using a quantum circuit with four logic qubits.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/challenge-classical-two-bit-adder?u=76281980&t=90)** The first two qubits represent our inputs A and B, and we can use a combination of poly-X gates to flip them into one of the four possible input states.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/challenge-classical-two-bit-adder?u=76281980&t=100)** The adder circuit will then modify the other two qubits representing the sum and carry-out bits, which are then measured at the end as output.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/challenge-classical-two-bit-adder?u=76281980&t=109)** Your job is to implement the adder circuit in the middle, and we've provided a template for this circuit in the exercise files that you can use as a starting point.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/challenge-classical-two-bit-adder?u=76281980&t=118)** Your quantum version of the two-bit adder should implement the same logic table, setting the values of the sum and carry-out bits accordingly.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/challenge-classical-two-bit-adder?u=76281980&t=126)** This challenge is a logic puzzle.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/challenge-classical-two-bit-adder?u=76281980&t=129)** Take some time to think it through and come up with your own solution, then watch the next video to see how I approach this problem.

#### Solution: Classical two-bit adder
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-classical-two-bit-adder?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-classical-two-bit-adder?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Let's talk through how I came to my solution for the challenge to build a two bit binary adder circuit using quantum logic gates.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-classical-two-bit-adder?u=76281980&t=13)** Looking at the logic table I decide to figure out how to produce each of the two output quibits independently.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-classical-two-bit-adder?u=76281980&t=20)** Consider the sum qubit first.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-classical-two-bit-adder?u=76281980&t=23)** I noticed that it's only one when both inputs A and B are different and that the sum was zero whenever both inputs were the same.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-classical-two-bit-adder?u=76281980&t=32)** Thinking about that, I realized I could implement that logic by using a pair of CNOT gates.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-classical-two-bit-adder?u=76281980&t=39)** The first CNOT on line six would use the first input qubit as its controlled to conditionally flip the sum qubit and then the second CNOT on line seven will use the second input qubit as its control for flipping the sum.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-classical-two-bit-adder?u=76281980&t=53)** If only one of the inputs is one then the sum will be flipped once ending up in the one state.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-classical-two-bit-adder?u=76281980&t=60)** However, if both inputs are one, then both CNOT gates would be triggered flipping the sum from zero to one and then from one back to zero.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-classical-two-bit-adder?u=76281980&t=69)** This circuit diagram makes it a little easier to see how those two CNOT gates use the first two qubits as controls to conditionally flip the third sum qubit.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-classical-two-bit-adder?u=76281980&t=80)** After solving that puzzle for the sum I turned my focus to the fourth carry out qubit.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-classical-two-bit-adder?u=76281980&t=86)** The carry out is only one when both inputs are also one.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-classical-two-bit-adder?u=76281980&t=90)** Otherwise, the carry out qubit is zero.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-classical-two-bit-adder?u=76281980&t=93)** Thinking about how to implement that I realized I need to flip the carry out qubit from zero to one if and only if both inputs are one and conveniently there's a quantum gate for just that.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-classical-two-bit-adder?u=76281980&t=107)** On line eight, I configured it Toffoli gate to use the first two input qubits as controls and flip the fourth carryout qubit accordingly.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-classical-two-bit-adder?u=76281980&t=117)** That produced the quantum circuit shown here which is my final solution to the challenge.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-classical-two-bit-adder?u=76281980&t=122)** This circuit diagram makes it a little easier to see how the two CNOT gates use the first two qubits as controls to conditionally flip the third sum qubit.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-classical-two-bit-adder?u=76281980&t=132)** This is my simple solution, but there are other combinations of quantum logic gates that could produce a similar result.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-classical-two-bit-adder?u=76281980&t=139)** If you approach this challenge differently please leave a comment.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-classical-two-bit-adder?u=76281980&t=142)** We'd love to see how you solved it.

### 6. Quantum Entanglement

#### What is quantum entanglement?
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/what-is-quantum-entanglement?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/what-is-quantum-entanglement?u=76281980&t=0)** - We've put qubits into lots of different quantum states throughout this course, but so far, in all of the states we've explored, each of those qubits could be described independently.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/what-is-quantum-entanglement?u=76281980&t=10)** - For example, the two qubits Barron and I are holding are in different states of superposition.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/what-is-quantum-entanglement?u=76281980&t=15)** This is a two-qubit system which we can represent with some state vector.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/what-is-quantum-entanglement?u=76281980&t=19)** - If we measure Olivia's qubit, it has some probability of being zero or one.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/what-is-quantum-entanglement?u=76281980&t=24)** But her probability amplitudes and the outcome of that measurement have no impact on my qubit.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/what-is-quantum-entanglement?u=76281980&t=30)** - Likewise, the quantum state or measurement outcome of Barron's qubit has nothing to do with mine.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/what-is-quantum-entanglement?u=76281980&t=35)** We can take Barron's qubit the picture and still describe the state of my qubit.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/what-is-quantum-entanglement?u=76281980&t=40)** Our two qubits are-
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/what-is-quantum-entanglement?u=76281980&t=42)** - [Both] Independent.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/what-is-quantum-entanglement?u=76281980&t=43)** - To really take advantage of quantum computing, we need to create a new type of connection between qubits.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/what-is-quantum-entanglement?u=76281980&t=49)** - Is it finally time?

> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/what-is-quantum-entanglement?u=76281980&t=53)** - [Both] Entanglement.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/what-is-quantum-entanglement?u=76281980&t=54)** - Quantum entanglement is a strange quantum phenomenon where two or more particles, or qubits, interact with each other in such a way that they become, well, entangled, meaning the quantum state of one qubit is dependent on the state of another qubit.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/what-is-quantum-entanglement?u=76281980&t=68)** For example, if we entangle two qubits in a state of superposition, the outcome of measuring one of those qubits becomes correlated with the observed state of the other qubit.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/what-is-quantum-entanglement?u=76281980&t=78)** We can entangle two qubits such that their measured states will always be the same, or we can entangle them such that they're anti-correlated, meaning their measured states will always be the opposite of each other.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/what-is-quantum-entanglement?u=76281980&t=91)** In either case, we do not know what the final measured states of those qubits will be, but we do know the relationship between them.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/what-is-quantum-entanglement?u=76281980&t=99)** The observed states of those cubits have become dependent on each other and we can no longer describe their states individually.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/what-is-quantum-entanglement?u=76281980&t=105)** - This idea of entanglement can be hard to grasp as humans because it's a quantum phenomenon that applies to tiny little particles and doesn't really relate to the large scale physical world as we experience it
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/what-is-quantum-entanglement?u=76281980&t=117)** - To explore the concept with an analogy that we can relate to as people, let's revisit the Quantum Cafe from earlier in this course where dining qubits can pick from one of two menu items, the soup or the salad.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/what-is-quantum-entanglement?u=76281980&t=130)** I have a qubit in a state of superposition undecided about whether to order the soup or salad.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/what-is-quantum-entanglement?u=76281980&t=135)** - And in this scenario, I am also a qubit in a state of decision superposition.

>

>

>

>
> **[5:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/what-is-quantum-entanglement?u=76281980&t=343)** - Well, for a quantum algorithm to be faster than its classical counterpart, it must use entanglement.
>
> **[5:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/what-is-quantum-entanglement?u=76281980&t=349)** In fact, it's been proven that a quantum algorithm that does not use entangled quantum states at some point along the way can be efficiently simulated using a regular classical computer.
>
> **[6:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/what-is-quantum-entanglement?u=76281980&t=360)** Without entanglement, there's no quantum advantage.

#### Represent entangled qubits
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-entangled-qubits?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-entangled-qubits?u=76281980&t=0)** - The recipe to create an entangled pair of qubits is fairly simple.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-entangled-qubits?u=76281980&t=4)** It only requires two ingredients.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-entangled-qubits?u=76281980&t=6)** Starting with both of our qubits in the |0&gt; basis state, I'll apply a Hadamard gate to spin my qubit into super position.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-entangled-qubits?u=76281980&t=14)** - And then I'll apply a CNOT gate with Baron's qubit as a control to decide whether or not to flip my target qubit.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-entangled-qubits?u=76281980&t=21)** - The combined quantum situation we've just created is what's known as a Bell state, which is the simplest and most extreme example of quantum entanglement.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-entangled-qubits?u=76281980&t=30)** - Since our entangled qubits can no longer be described independently, the states you're seeing on our block spheres are not really correct, so to visually indicate that our qubits are entangled, we'll use this phi symbol, which represents a Bell state.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-entangled-qubits?u=76281980&t=48)** - Now that we're entangled, if we measure my qubit first, (Velcro rustles) and see that its state is 0-
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-entangled-qubits?u=76281980&t=54)** - Then that means my target qubit is unchanged.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-entangled-qubits?u=76281980&t=57)** I'm still in the 0 state.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-entangled-qubits?u=76281980&t=59)** (Velcro rustles)
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-entangled-qubits?u=76281980&t=60)** - But going back a few seconds, if when we measured my qubit, (Velcro rustles) we saw an outcome of 1-
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-entangled-qubits?u=76281980&t=67)** - Then that means my target qubit would've been flipped and is therefore (Velcro rustles) 1 as well.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-entangled-qubits?u=76281980&t=73)** In both of those scenarios, we ended up with the same outcome because we're entangled.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-entangled-qubits?u=76281980&t=78)** - It's tempting to describe what just happened as a causal series of events related to the CNOT gate that was connecting us.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-entangled-qubits?u=76281980&t=85)** When my control qubit was measured as 1, did that event cause Olivia's target qubit to flip to 1 as well?
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-entangled-qubits?u=76281980&t=92)** Whereas in the first scenario, when my control qubit was measured as 0, did that cause Olivia's target qubit to be left alone and stay 0.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-entangled-qubits?u=76281980&t=102)** As humans, that's how we might try to rationalize what's happening here, but that's not really how entanglement works.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-entangled-qubits?u=76281980&t=109)** As we mentioned in a previous video, scientific experiments have shown there's not some time-traveling secret communication going on between our qubits.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-entangled-qubits?u=76281980&t=117)** This connection is part of the strange and spooky nature of quantum mechanics.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-entangled-qubits?u=76281980&t=121)** - Fortunately, even if it's hard to wrap our human minds around entanglement, we can describe the phenomenon mathematically.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-entangled-qubits?u=76281980&t=128)** Starting with a pair of qubits in the |00&gt; basis state, we'll use the Kronecker product of the Hadamard and Identity gates to apply the Hadamard operation to the left qubit.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-entangled-qubits?u=76281980&t=138)** This rotates it into the ket plus superposition, which gives us this four-element state vector with two non-zero probability amplitudes.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-entangled-qubits?u=76281980&t=147)** Notice we've factored out the 1 over square root of 2 and put it in front of these expressions to make them easier to read.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-entangled-qubits?u=76281980&t=154)** If we measure this pair of qubits, there's a one in two chance we'll get an outcome of |00&gt;, and one in two chance it will be |10&gt;, and that leaves zero probability it'll be anything else.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-entangled-qubits?u=76281980&t=167)** At this point, our two qubits are still completely independent and have no effect on each other.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-entangled-qubits?u=76281980&t=172)** The red qubit will always collapse to 0, and the fact that the right qubit is always 0 has no effect on the left qubit's outcome.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-entangled-qubits?u=76281980&t=180)** Now let's take that state.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-entangled-qubits?u=76281980&t=182)** We'll call it |+0, and let's apply a CNOT gate using the left qubit that we spun with the Hadamard gate as a control to determine whether or not to flip the right qubit from its |0&gt; state.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-entangled-qubits?u=76281980&t=194)** Looking at the resulting state vector, we can see that the location of the probability amplitudes have changed.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-entangled-qubits?u=76281980&t=201)** Now, we have equal 1/2 probabilities that the measured outcomes will be the same, either |00&gt;, or |11&gt;, and no chance that the two outcomes will be different.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-entangled-qubits?u=76281980&t=212)** That means if we measure either one of these qubits, we'll immediately know what the outcome will be when we measure the other.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-entangled-qubits?u=76281980&t=218)** Their outcomes are correlated to always be the same.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/represent-entangled-qubits?u=76281980&t=221)** Entanglement is one of the strange aspects of quantum computing that often makes the most sense when we describe it in terms of math.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Speakers:** - the (2), - then (2), - and (1), - since (1), - now (1)
> **Code Keywords:** let (2), super (1), else. (1)
> **Env Vars:** cnot (3)
> **Cross-References:** we mentioned (1), previous video (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (1)
> **Definitions:** known as (1)

#### Simulate a bell state with Qiskit
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/simulate-a-bell-state-with-qiskit?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/simulate-a-bell-state-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] To explore quantum entanglement in Qiskit, let's simulate the Bell States, which are a specific set of quantum states in which two qubits are maximally entangled.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/simulate-a-bell-state-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=10)** There are two Bell states where the qubits are entangled such that they will always have the same measured outcomes.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/simulate-a-bell-state-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=16)** And there are two Bell states in which the two qubits will always have the opposite measurement outcomes.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/simulate-a-bell-state-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=21)** Together these four quantum states are an orthonormal basis that can be used to represent a two-qubit state known as the Bell basis.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/simulate-a-bell-state-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=30)** Keep that in mind because we'll use this Bell basis when we explore a few quantum algorithms near the end of this course.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/simulate-a-bell-state-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=37)** For this demonstration, we'll start with a simple program that creates an empty two-qubit circuit.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/simulate-a-bell-state-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=42)** This program displays the circuit statevector and then the final notebook cell simulates measuring both of these qubits to display the outcomes on a histogram.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/simulate-a-bell-state-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=52)** Now, to put these qubits into a Bell state, let's apply a Hadamard gate to the first qubit, and then apply a CNOT gate with the first qubit as the control and the second qubit as the target.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/simulate-a-bell-state-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=71)** I'll press the Run All button to run this notebook.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/simulate-a-bell-state-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=75)** Now we can see the circuit that the code built with the Hadamard gate followed by a CNOT.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/simulate-a-bell-state-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=81)** The resulting statevector, which has equal probability amplitudes of 1 over the square root of 2 in the first and last positions, indicate the measured outcomes of the two bits will always be the same.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/simulate-a-bell-state-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=92)** And below that, the simulated histogram shows that roughly half the time we measured 00 and the other half of the time we measured 11.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/simulate-a-bell-state-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=101)** We never saw an outcome where the two bits were different.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/simulate-a-bell-state-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=104)** Creating a Bell state is that simple.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/simulate-a-bell-state-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=107)** Now the order in which the two gates are applied matters.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/simulate-a-bell-state-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=110)** Let's use cut and paste to move the Hadamard gate to after the CNOT.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/simulate-a-bell-state-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=119)** I'll rerun those notebook cells.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/simulate-a-bell-state-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=126)** And now looking at the statevector and histogram we see a different set of outputs.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/simulate-a-bell-state-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=131)** The first qubit has equal odds of either outcome, while the second qubit is always measured as 0.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/simulate-a-bell-state-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=137)** By making CNOT the first gate in the circuit, at that point in time, the first control qubit was still zero, so the CNOT did nothing.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/simulate-a-bell-state-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=146)** Then afterward, the Hadamard gate put the first qubit into superposition, so the order matters.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/simulate-a-bell-state-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=152)** Let's undo that change.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/simulate-a-bell-state-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=161)** And now let's add another gate to our circuit.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/simulate-a-bell-state-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=164)** I'll insert a Pauli-X gate to flip the second qubit before we use the CNOT gate to entangle them.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/simulate-a-bell-state-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=173)** I'll rerun those notebook cells one more time.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/simulate-a-bell-state-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=177)** And now we've created a different Bell state than before.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/simulate-a-bell-state-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=180)** The statevector shows that the two non-zero probability amplitudes are now the middle two elements, and the histogram shows that the two measured states are 01 and 10.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/simulate-a-bell-state-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=192)** And now we've entangled our qubits to have an anti-correlated outcome, meaning they're measured states will always be the opposite of each other.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/simulate-a-bell-state-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=200)** We encourage you to play around with this example and create other circuit variations to put two qubits into entangled states.

#### Challenge: Entangle three qubits
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/challenge-entangle-three-qubits?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/challenge-entangle-three-qubits?u=76281980&t=0)** - It's time for another challenge to practice entangling some qubits.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/challenge-entangle-three-qubits?u=76281980&t=10)** Your goal for this challenge is to build a quantum circuit that puts three qubits into what's known as the GHZ state.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/challenge-entangle-three-qubits?u=76281980&t=17)** The Greenberger-Horne-Zeilinger or GHZ state is a specific type of entangled quantum state involving at least three qubits.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/challenge-entangle-three-qubits?u=76281980&t=26)** The state vector for a three qubit GHZ state is shown here and we can see that those qubits have an equal probability of all being measured as zero or all being measured as one.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/challenge-entangle-three-qubits?u=76281980&t=38)** All three of them will always have the same outcome and will never end up with a mixed measurement outcome.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/challenge-entangle-three-qubits?u=76281980&t=43)** You can think of this as an extension of the two qubit Bell state we saw earlier in a previous video, but using more qubits.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/challenge-entangle-three-qubits?u=76281980&t=50)** We've included a template in the exercise files that you can use as a starting point.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/challenge-entangle-three-qubits?u=76281980&t=54)** There are multiple ways to achieve this desired GHZ state.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/challenge-entangle-three-qubits?u=76281980&t=58)** So take some time to build and test your own solution.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/challenge-entangle-three-qubits?u=76281980&t=61)** Then watch the next video to see how we approached it.

#### Solution: Entangle three qubits
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-entangle-three-qubits?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-entangle-three-qubits?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Let's look at how I entangled three qubits in the GHZ state in which all three qubits will always have the same measurement outcome.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-entangle-three-qubits?u=76281980&t=14)** Starting from this empty circuit with three qubits, the first thing I did was entangle the first two qubits to create a Bell state between them.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-entangle-three-qubits?u=76281980&t=22)** To do that, I applied a Hadamard gate to the first qubit, and after that, I applied a CNOT gate with the first qubit as the control and the second qubit as its target.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-entangle-three-qubits?u=76281980&t=36)** I'll click the Run All arrow to re-run this notebook.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-entangle-three-qubits?u=76281980&t=41)** And we can see from the output histogram that those first two qubits are always the same and the third qubit is always zero, which makes sense because we haven't done anything to it yet.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-entangle-three-qubits?u=76281980&t=52)** Now, to pull that third qubit into entanglement with the first two qubits, I'll add another CNOT gate to the circuit, (keyboard clacking) using the second qubit as the control and the third qubit as the target.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-entangle-three-qubits?u=76281980&t=68)** I'll run those notebook cells again.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-entangle-three-qubits?u=76281980&t=71)** Now that second CNOT connects the final qubit to the Bell state of the first two qubits creating an entangled dependency between all three of them.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-entangle-three-qubits?u=76281980&t=81)** The statevector shows equal non-zero probability amplitudes in its first and last elements, and that corresponds to the simulated results in the histogram which show roughly equal outcomes of 0, 0, 0, or 1, 1, 1, and nothing else.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-entangle-three-qubits?u=76281980&t=99)** Now, when connecting that third qubit to the first two entangled qubits in the Bell state, I used the middle qubit as the CNOT's control; however, I could have used either of those first two qubits as the control because once they're entangled in the Bell state, there's nothing to distinguish one as being the controller of the other.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-entangle-three-qubits?u=76281980&t=119)** So let's change the control bit on that second CNOT gate to use the first qubit as its control.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-entangle-three-qubits?u=76281980&t=126)** I'll re-run the notebook one more time, and we can see the updated circuit diagram.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-entangle-three-qubits?u=76281980&t=134)** Scrolling down.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-entangle-three-qubits?u=76281980&t=136)** Notice that the statevector is exactly the same as before.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-entangle-three-qubits?u=76281980&t=139)** It doesn't matter which of those two qubits in the Bell-state pair we used as the control when bringing the third qubit into entanglement with them, the resulting statevector is the same.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-entangle-three-qubits?u=76281980&t=150)** And even though the histogram may have changed a little bit due to randomness in the simulation, it still shows roughly equal outcomes of 0, 0, 0, and 1, 1, 1, and nothing else.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-entangle-three-qubits?u=76281980&t=162)** If you enjoyed this challenge and want to take your quantum exploration further, we have a couple of activities to recommend as bonus challenges.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-entangle-three-qubits?u=76281980&t=171)** The simplest would be to extend your solution to the three-qubit GHZ state to entangle four or more qubits in a similar manner where all of their measured outcomes are the same.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-entangle-three-qubits?u=76281980&t=182)** And if you want an even tougher bonus challenge, then try to build a quantum circuit that puts three qubits into what's known as the W state, which has an equal probability of the three outcomes shown here in which only one of the three measured qubits will be in the one state.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-entangle-three-qubits?u=76281980&t=198)** Putting three qubits into this W state is more complicated than the GHZ state circuit we just saw, but I encourage you to take on the challenge of figuring it out if you're looking for extra practice.

### 7. Real Quantum Hardware
