>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/access-ibm-quantum-computers?u=76281980&t=118)** We can also see that there are several cloud-based simulators available for us to use as well.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/access-ibm-quantum-computers?u=76281980&t=124)** I'll click on the system named Oslo, and that brings up this page with additional information.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/access-ibm-quantum-computers?u=76281980&t=132)** The first thing you may notice is the map view, which shows the connectivity between the seven qubits on this processor.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/access-ibm-quantum-computers?u=76281980&t=138)** They're arranged like the letter H, and if we look at the name of this type of processor, we can see that it's a R5.11H.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/access-ibm-quantum-computers?u=76281980&t=147)** If we look at another processor, I'll select Lima, which is a Falcon R4T, we can see that its five qubits are arranged like the letter T.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/access-ibm-quantum-computers?u=76281980&t=157)** In a real quantum processor, not every qubit is connected to every other qubit, which means only certain pairs of qubits can perform operations together such as a CNOT gate.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/access-ibm-quantum-computers?u=76281980&t=167)** In this processor, qubits zero and one are connected together so they can perform a CNOT together.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/access-ibm-quantum-computers?u=76281980&t=174)** However, qubits zero and two are not directly connected, so they cannot execute a CNOT.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/access-ibm-quantum-computers?u=76281980&t=181)** These limitations can be important to consider when writing programs to run on real quantum hardware, but for the scope of this course, we won't worry about it.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/access-ibm-quantum-computers?u=76281980&t=190)** When we write our programs in Qiskit and target them to run on a real processor, under the hood, Qiskit will take into account those connectivity limitations when it transpiles the program.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/access-ibm-quantum-computers?u=76281980&t=201)** It may change the mapping of physical bits or even insert additional operations to achieve the desired logic we specify in our program.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/access-ibm-quantum-computers?u=76281980&t=209)** Those details can impact performance when the program runs, but again, diving into that is beyond the scope of this course.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/access-ibm-quantum-computers?u=76281980&t=217)** We can see that this system named Lima was last calibrated about 19 hours ago, and if you're curious to explore that calibration data, you can do so by looking at the table view.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/access-ibm-quantum-computers?u=76281980&t=228)** Now, shifting focus to the section above that, we can see that this processor currently has 519 pending jobs.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/access-ibm-quantum-computers?u=76281980&t=236)** Keep in mind, these systems are shared resources, so when you submit your program to run, it will go into a job queue, and you may end up waiting for a while to get the results back.
>
> **[4:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/access-ibm-quantum-computers?u=76281980&t=246)** Finally, notice that this processor only has five basis gates.
>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/access-ibm-quantum-computers?u=76281980&t=250)** CX, which is the controlled X or CNOT gate, ID, which is the identity gate that does nothing to a qubit for one unit of time, the RZ gate, which rotates the qubit state around the Z-axis by a given angle, SX, which is a gate we have not covered in this course.
>
> **[4:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/access-ibm-quantum-computers?u=76281980&t=268)** It's the square root of the X gate, which rotates the quantum state by 90 degrees around the x-axis, and finally, the regular poly-x gate.
>
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/access-ibm-quantum-computers?u=76281980&t=277)** These are the only five gates that this quantum processor can physically execute.
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/access-ibm-quantum-computers?u=76281980&t=282)** However, this small set of gates is all we need to implement the logic of all of the other gates we've explored throughout this course.
>
> **[4:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/access-ibm-quantum-computers?u=76281980&t=290)** When you tell your Qiskit program to target this processor, under the hood, the transpiler will implement the logic of your program using just these five gates.
>
> **[5:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/access-ibm-quantum-computers?u=76281980&t=300)** Different quantum processors built by different companies may use different sets of basis gates to implement all of the logic operations.

#### Mitigate quantum measurement errors
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/mitigate-quantum-measurement-errors?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/mitigate-quantum-measurement-errors?u=76281980&t=0)** - It seems like quantum computers have a lot of computational errors due to noise and other factors.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/mitigate-quantum-measurement-errors?u=76281980&t=5)** Is there anything we can do to help reduce, or mitigate those errors?
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/mitigate-quantum-measurement-errors?u=76281980&t=9)** - The best solution would be to build better quantum computers with less noise, or unwanted randomness.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/mitigate-quantum-measurement-errors?u=76281980&t=14)** And quantum researchers are working towards that goal every day, but physics... It's a hard problem to solve and the best quantum computers we have today still have a lot of noise and errors because they're physical systems working in delicate quantum states.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/mitigate-quantum-measurement-errors?u=76281980&t=30)** - Well, what if we know something about the errors a specific quantum computer produces?
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/mitigate-quantum-measurement-errors?u=76281980&t=35)** If we knew the chances of measuring the wrong outcome from a known state, couldn't we use that information to fix the results of an unknown computation?
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/mitigate-quantum-measurement-errors?u=76281980&t=42)** - Now you're onto something.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/mitigate-quantum-measurement-errors?u=76281980&t=44)** One way to characterize a specific quantum computer is with a set of simple quantum circuits.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/mitigate-quantum-measurement-errors?u=76281980&t=49)** For example, in a two-qubit system, if we simulate the circuit that immediately measures both qubits in their initial state we'll always see 00 as the outcome.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/mitigate-quantum-measurement-errors?u=76281980&t=60)** However, on a real quantum computer, we'll see results like this with the 00 state occurring most of the time, but with a small number of results in the other states.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/mitigate-quantum-measurement-errors?u=76281980&t=71)** Along the same lines, this simple calibration circuit, which flips the first qubit before measurement, should always produce an output of 01 in theory.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/mitigate-quantum-measurement-errors?u=76281980&t=80)** On a real quantum computer, we'll see 01 most of the time, but the other states will also show up in the results.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/mitigate-quantum-measurement-errors?u=76281980&t=87)** We can characterize a two-qubit system using these four calibration circuits, which put those two qubits into each of the four possible basis states, and then immediately measure them.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/mitigate-quantum-measurement-errors?u=76281980&t=97)** Running these circuits on a specific quantum computer will give us insight into how often we see each of those other wrong results due to noise and other factors.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/mitigate-quantum-measurement-errors?u=76281980&t=106)** It takes those four calibration circuits to characterize a two-qubit system.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/mitigate-quantum-measurement-errors?u=76281980&t=111)** We would need eight calibration circuits to characterize a three-qubit system.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/mitigate-quantum-measurement-errors?u=76281980&t=115)** 16 for a four-qubit system, and so on.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/mitigate-quantum-measurement-errors?u=76281980&t=118)** Using those calibration results, it's possible to build a filter that we can apply to the measured outcomes of another quantum computation to counteract, or undo the errors that occur in that physical system.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/mitigate-quantum-measurement-errors?u=76281980&t=130)** It's not a perfect process, but it can help improve our results.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/mitigate-quantum-measurement-errors?u=76281980&t=134)** - We won't be going into the details of how that works here because mitigating errors in quantum computation is a field of study that deserves an entire course of its own.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/mitigate-quantum-measurement-errors?u=76281980&t=143)** If you want to dive deeper into mitigating measurement errors in quantum circuits the Qiskit textbook is a great starting point which shows you how to use Qiskit packages to build your own air correcting filters.

### 8. Quantum Algorithms

#### Superdense coding
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/superdense-coding?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/superdense-coding?u=76281980&t=0)** - We've seen lots of single and multi-qubit logic gates and explored key quantum concepts, like super position and entanglement.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/superdense-coding?u=76281980&t=8)** Now it's finally time to put all those building blocks together to write quantum algorithms that do real work.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/superdense-coding?u=76281980&t=15)** - Our first algorithm is known as superdense coding.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/superdense-coding?u=76281980&t=18)** This is a quantum communication protocol that securely sends classical bits of information by transmitting a smaller number of quantum bits.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/superdense-coding?u=76281980&t=27)** This protocol starts with the sender and receiver each having half of an entangled pair of qubits before the message needs to be sent.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/superdense-coding?u=76281980&t=35)** Let's say I want to send two classical bits of information to Baron.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/superdense-coding?u=76281980&t=39)** The first thing we need is a pair of entangled qubits.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/superdense-coding?u=76281980&t=42)** - [Todd] Here you go!
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/superdense-coding?u=76281980&t=44)** - Thanks, Todd!
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/superdense-coding?u=76281980&t=47)** - It doesn't matter where these entangled qubits come from.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/superdense-coding?u=76281980&t=50)** I could have created them-
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/superdense-coding?u=76281980&t=51)** - Or I could have entangled them-
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/superdense-coding?u=76281980&t=53)** - Or they could have come from a third party.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/superdense-coding?u=76281980&t=56)** These two qubits are entangled in a simple bell state in which measuring them will always produce similar outcomes.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/superdense-coding?u=76281980&t=63)** Now, Olivia and I can each take one of these qubits and travel to opposite ends of the galaxy.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/superdense-coding?u=76281980&t=68)** - Let's just stick with opposite ends of the table for now.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/superdense-coding?u=76281980&t=72)** - That's reasonable.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/superdense-coding?u=76281980&t=75)** - Now that we're apart, to send my message to Baron, I'll perform one of four quantum operations on my qubit that I'm holding to encode two classical bits of information I want to send.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/superdense-coding?u=76281980&t=86)** If I want to send the two bit message, zero, zero, I'll use the identity gate and simply not do anything.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/superdense-coding?u=76281980&t=93)** That leaves our entangled pair in the same bell state that they started in.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/superdense-coding?u=76281980&t=97)** To send the message zero, one, I'll apply the Z gate to my qubit, which puts our entangled par into a different bell state.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/superdense-coding?u=76281980&t=104)** For the two bit message one, zero, I'll apply the X gate, putting our cubits in yet another bell state.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/superdense-coding?u=76281980&t=110)** And finally, to send the message one, one, I'll apply a Z gate followed by an X gate, which puts us in the fourth and final bell state.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/superdense-coding?u=76281980&t=119)** So our entangled cubits will be in one of these four bell basis states depending on the message.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/superdense-coding?u=76281980&t=126)** I want to send Baron the message one, zero, so I applied an X gate to shift our entangled qubits to the corresponding state.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/superdense-coding?u=76281980&t=133)** Now that I've encoded my message, I can send my qubit to Baron.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/superdense-coding?u=76281980&t=138)** - Thank you.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/superdense-coding?u=76281980&t=140)** To receive Olivia's message, I need to measure which of those four bell basis states our qubits are in.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/superdense-coding?u=76281980&t=146)** If I simply perform a standard Z measurement on these two qubits as is, the outcome I get will only tell me if they were entangled in a correlated bell state or an anti-correlated bell state.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/superdense-coding?u=76281980&t=158)** That's some information, but it's not enough to tell me which of the four possible messages Olivia intended to send.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/superdense-coding?u=76281980&t=164)** So before I measure these qubits, I need to decode her message.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/superdense-coding?u=76281980&t=169)** I'll do that by applying a couple of operations to unentangle our bell state pair, basically doing the reverse of the operations that were originally used to entangle these qubits.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/superdense-coding?u=76281980&t=179)** There's not a good way to physically show that with beach balls, so I'll represent that process by removing these bell state stickers.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/superdense-coding?u=76281980&t=188)** Now, these qubits are in independent basis states, and when I measure them, the outcome I get is Olivia's message, one, zero, and this outcome is deterministic.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/superdense-coding?u=76281980&t=199)** I will always see Olivia's message that she intended to send.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/superdense-coding?u=76281980&t=203)** Of course, that's ignoring other real world physical factors that might cause errors.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/superdense-coding?u=76281980&t=208)** - With superdense coding, I was able to send Baron two classical bits of information by only sending him one encoded quantum bit.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/superdense-coding?u=76281980&t=215)** - However, this protocol still involves two qubits.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/superdense-coding?u=76281980&t=219)** I can't extract two bits of information from either one of our single qubits.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/superdense-coding?u=76281980&t=223)** I need them both to decode Olivia's message.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/superdense-coding?u=76281980&t=227)** - That's why superdense coding is a form of secure communication.
>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/superdense-coding?u=76281980&t=231)** If a nefarious eavesdropper intercepted my qubit on its way to Baron and looks at it, the result they see is random and tells them nothing about my secret message.
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/superdense-coding?u=76281980&t=240)** - The fact that I needed both qubits to decode Olivia's message also means that we're not able to transmit information faster than the speed of light, which would break the laws of known physics.
>
> **[4:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/superdense-coding?u=76281980&t=251)** When Olivia applies one of the four quantum operations to encode her message, that has an instantaneous effect on my qubit, no matter how far apart we are because our qubits are entangled.
>
> **[4:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/superdense-coding?u=76281980&t=262)** - But without my qubit, Baron can't decode the message yet, so we haven't actually transferred any information.
>
> **[4:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/superdense-coding?u=76281980&t=269)** - This superdense coding protocol was first proposed in the 1970s, but it wasn't actually published until 1992 and then demonstrated in 1996 with an experiment using entangled photons.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/superdense-coding?u=76281980&t=281)** - In terms of scientific research, 1992 isn't that long ago.
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/superdense-coding?u=76281980&t=286)** - I find it inspiring to realize that such a simple protocol is only a few decades old.
>
> **[4:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/superdense-coding?u=76281980&t=291)** It makes you wonder what other elegant quantum algorithms are yet to be discovered.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Speakers:** - or (2), - that (2), - we (1), - our (1), - [todd] (1)
> **Code Keywords:** let (2), super (1), from. (1), finally, (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (3), known as (1)
> **CLI Commands:** find (1)

#### Quantum teleportation
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=0)** - We use superdense coding to securely transmit classical bits of information.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=5)** Now let's transmit a quantum state from one qubit to another with quantum teleportation.

>

> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=37)** - So it's like copy and paste, but for quantum computers.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=40)** - Kind of, but not really.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=42)** In a classical computer, it's easy to copy information.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=46)** We simply read the state of a bit that we want to copy and then send another bit to that state.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=51)** However, that approach doesn't work in a quantum computer because...
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=54)** - Because when you look at the qubit you want to copy, you are implicitly measuring it, which destroys the quantum state you want to transfer.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=63)** - Bingo, the rules are different with a quantum computer.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=70)** Let's say I want to transfer or teleport the quantum state of this qubit to Baron.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=75)** - Can you just give me that qubit?
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=78)** That seems like the simplest solution.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=80)** - No, this is my special qubit, and you can't physically have it, but I'll give you its state.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=86)** To do that, we'll need a pair of entangled qubits.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=89)** - Here you go. - Ah, thanks Todd.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=94)** - The quantum teleportation algorithm involves three qubits.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=97)** My qubit is in an unknown state that we want to transfer to one of Baron's qubits.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=102)** - Olivia has half of a bell state pair and it doesn't matter which one since the state is symmetric.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=109)** Now, we can be light years apart and we'll still be able to share quantum information through this entangled connection.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=116)** - For the next step in the teleportation algorithm, I'll perform a bell state measurement on my qubit and the qubit Baron sent me.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=123)** To do that, I'll apply the C knot and head mar gate, and then I'll measure both qubits.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=132)** This is a partial measurement of our three qubit system which gives us two bits of classical information.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=137)** Now I need to send those results to Baron using any classical communication method, so I'll send him a text.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=145)** - Got it.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=146)** Now, for the last step in this algorithm, I'll apply one of four transformations to my qubit based on the result from Olivia's measurement, and that will fully recover the teleported state.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=157)** If both of the qubits Olivia measured were zero, that means the qubit I'm holding is already in the correct and final state, so I don't need to do anything to it.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=165)** I can use an identity gate for that.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=168)** If Olivia measures her half of our shared bell pair as one and her original qubit as zero, then I'll need to apply and X gate.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=176)** If Olivia measures just her qubit as one, then I'll need to apply a Z gate, and if she measures both of those qubits as one, then I'll need to apply an X gate followed by a Z gate.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=188)** To understand why this set of four operations recovers the teleported state requires a deep dive into the math behind the algorithm, which is beyond the scope for this course.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=197)** If you're interested in understanding those details, then we recommend reading the page about quantum teleportation from the Qiskit textbook.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=204)** Since Olivia measured her qubit as zero and the middle shared qubit as one, I applied in X gate to my qubit, which puts it into the state that Olivia's qubit was originally in.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=216)** - We've successfully teleport the quantum state from my qubit to Baron's qubit.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=220)** Notice that as part of that process, I had to measure my qubit, so it's no longer in its original quantum state.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=227)** This was a transfer of information, we did not copy it.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=230)** - Since we used entanglement to transfer that state and the correlation between entangled qubits is instantaneous, it may seem like we just sent information between us faster than the speed of light, which would defy the known rules of physics.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=245)** But remember, I needed to know Olivia's measurement results to know which of the four possible operations to apply.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=253)** If I had measured my qubit before I received Olivia's results and performed that final recovery step, the outcome I get would be random and meaningless to me.
>
> **[4:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=262)** I can't extract any useful information from that random result so no information has actually been shared.
>
> **[4:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=269)** - Along those lines, when I performed that bell state measurement on the two qubits I was holding, each of the four possible outcomes had an equal probability of occurring.
>
> **[4:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=279)** If someone intercepted those two classical bits of information I texted to Baron, that would not give them any information about the quantum state we're transferring.
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=288)** To an eavesdropper, those are just two random bits.
>
> **[4:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=291)** - To complete the process, I need both my physical qubit and those two bits of classical information to apply the right final operation.
>
> **[5:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=301)** This quantum teleportation algorithm is a close relative of the superdense coding algorithm we looked at in an earlier video.
>
> **[5:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=308)** With superdense coding, we transferred two classical bits of information by encoding the message using an entangled qubit and then physically sending that one qubit to the receiver to be decoded.
>
> **[5:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=320)** With quantum teleportation, we transferred information about one qubit's quantum state through a process that involved physically sending two classical bits of information to the receiver to be decoded.
>
> **[5:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=332)** A commonality in both of these algorithms is that the sender and receiver share an entangled pair of qubits.
>
> **[5:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=338)** - Like superdense coding, quantum teleportation was discovered and experimentally demonstrated in the mid 1990s.
>
> **[5:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=345)** - That's within my lifetime. - Mine too.
>
> **[5:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=347)** And that's why I like to think of them as young algorithms.
>
> **[5:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=351)** - It's possible to extend the simple version of the protocol we saw in this video to teleport more complex quantum systems.
>
> **[5:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=358)** So it's a good algorithm to have in your bag of quantum tools.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Speakers:** - we (2), - so (2), - the (2), - ooh (1), - not (1)
> **Code Keywords:** let (2)
> **Definitions:** is a  (2)
> **Analogies:** it's like (1)

#### Quantum teleportation with Qiskit
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation-with-qiskit?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Let's look at an example quantum teleportation circuit with Qiskit.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=4)** After the initial import statements, the second cell of this notebook initializes a quantum circuit with three quantum registers and two classical registers.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=14)** We've used the optional name parameter when defining these registers to give them unique names to help identify them.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=20)** Qubit zero is Olivia's qubit, whose state we want to teleport.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=25)** Qubit one is the half of our Bell state pair that gets sent to Olivia, and qubit two is Barron's qubit, which is the other half of that Bell state pair and the destination for our teleportation.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=36)** We've named the last two classical registers CRZ and CRX because we'll be using the measured results stored in them to conditionally apply Z and X gates to Barron's target qubit.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=48)** Next, after creating the circuit, we initialize Olivia's qubit to the quantum state we'll be transferring.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=55)** In practice, we likely won't know the state of the qubit we're teleporting, but for the sake of this demonstration, we use the RX gate at line 12 to apply an arbitrary rotation to put Olivia's qubit into a super-positioned state.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=69)** After that, lines 16 and 17 use a Hadamard gate and CNOT to create a Bell state pair, and then on lines 21 through 25, Olivia performs a Bell state measurement on her qubit and half of that entangled pair.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=83)** Finally, lines 29 and 30 conditionally apply the X and or Z gates based on the results of Olivia's measurements, which are stored in the CRX and CRZ registers.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=96)** We can use Qiskit's State Vector Simulator to visualize the in-state of our three qubits on the block sphere.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=103)** What we're seeing here shows qubits zero and one after performing the Bell state measurement, so they've collapsed into one of the four two bit basis states with an equal probability of each outcome.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=114)** This time, Q zero was in the zero state, and Q1 ended up in the one state.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=119)** However, at the end of the teleportation algorithm, Barron's qubit, which is the qubit on the far right, will always be in the state that Olivia's qubit, qubit zero, was at the start.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=131)** Since we used a rotational X gate to initialize Olivia's qubit, now at the end, Barron's qubit is in that specific super position.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=140)** If I selected that cell and press control enter several times to rerun that simulation,
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=152)** we get a random result each time for the first two qubits, but qubit two will always end in the teleported state.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=160)** Now, let's scroll back up and change line 12 from RX to just a simple X gate, which will initialize Olivia's qubit in the one state.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=175)** I'll press shift enter twice to rebuild that circuit and run the state vector simulation, and now we see that the one state has been teleported from Q zero to Q2.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=189)** To measure our teleported quantum state, the next section of code adds a third classical register named result to our circuit, then measures the state of Barron's qubit, Q2, into that result register.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=201)** I'll press shift enter to run that cell and draw the new circuit, and I'll also run the next cell, which simulates that circuit and plots the counts on a histogram.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=217)** Looking at the results, we see four outcomes for that three qubit system.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=222)** We can see that, as expected, Q2 was always measured as one because that's the state we teleported, and the other two qubits were measured in one of their four possible combinations roughly the same number of times.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=235)** Now, there's a potential issue with the way we've implemented this algorithm, and that's because we've included instructions after performing a measurement.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=245)** That's not a problem for the Qiskit simulator, but real world quantum hardware may not allow additional instructions to be executed after it performs a measurement.
>
> **[4:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=254)** In fact, if we tried to run this algorithm in its current form using one of IBM's quantum computers, the job would fail.
>
> **[4:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=262)** Fortunately, there's a quantum computing principle known as deferred measurement, which states that any measurement can be postponed until the end of the circuit.
>
> **[4:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=272)** We won't dive into the details behind that principle, but in short, it means that waiting until the very end of a quantum computation to perform all of the measurements will not affect the probability distribution of the outcomes.
>
> **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=285)** To demonstrate that, we've created this modified version of the teleportation circuit, which is included at the bottom of that Jupyter Notebook.
>
> **[4:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=293)** It starts out similar to the previous circuit but instead of conditionally executing X and Z gates based on the result of the Bell state measurement, line 19 executes a controlled X gate using the middle qubit as the control and Barron's qubit as the target.
>
> **[5:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=309)** Then line 20 performs a controlled Z gate using the state of Olivia's qubit to determine whether or not to apply a Z gate to Barron's target qubit.
>
> **[5:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=319)** We haven't seen the controlled Z gate in this course, but as its name implies, it's basically the same as a controlled X gate.
>
> **[5:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=326)** It just applies a Z gate to the target instead of X.
>
> **[5:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=330)** Even though we haven't performed measurements on Olivia's qubits, those two controlled gates perform the right transformation on Barron's qubit to recover the teleported state.
>
> **[5:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=340)** At the end of this example circuit, we only need to measure Barron's destination qubit, and if we look at the simulation results for that circuit, we can see that the outcome is always one.
>
> **[5:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=351)** That makes sense because we initialized Olivia's qubit to the one state using an X gate.
>
> **[5:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=357)** This modified version of the circuit, which defers the measurement, enables us to run and demonstrate the algorithm on real hardware like IBM's quantum computers.
>
> **[6:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=367)** This circuit can transfer the state from one quantum register to another within a quantum processor.
>
> **[6:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=373)** However, it's not really the same as the original teleportation circuit we looked at because we're no longer transferring the quantum state via classical channels.
>
> **[6:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/quantum-teleportation-with-qiskit?u=76281980&t=383)** If we want to teleport a quantum state across great distances, then the first circuit best represents the actual protocol we need to do that, taking a Bell state measurement and then transmitting those classical results to the receiver to perform the final recovery operation.

#### Solution: Real quantum teleportation
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-real-quantum-teleportation?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-real-quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] To solve the challenge of teleporting a qubit, I started by copying the example teleportation code from an earlier video.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-real-quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=13)** Since the end goal is to run this algorithm on real quantum hardware, I picked the variation that defers measurement until the end.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-real-quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=21)** Now, the circuit I copied from that earlier video teleports the state of qubit zero to qubit two, but this challenge asked us to go in the opposite direction, teleporting the state of qubit two to qubit zero after initializing qubit two with a Hadamard gate.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-real-quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=39)** The simplest way to modify this circuit to meet that goal will be to swap around the indices of these various operations, to flip around the source and destination of the teleportation.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-real-quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=49)** Let's start with that approach on line five by changing the initialization gate from Pauli X to a Hadamard gate and then changing the index from zero to two.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-real-quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=62)** I'll continue that process on the next section, which prepares the bell state by changing the target index on the CNOT gate from two to zero.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-real-quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=71)** And for the bell state measurement, we'll change the indices on lines 14 and 15 from zero to two.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-real-quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=79)** We'll swap around the indices for the conditional X and Z gates near the end of the algorithm.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-real-quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=88)** And finally, we'll modify the measurement to look at qubit zero and store the result in the single classical register.

> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-real-quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=96)** Those are all the modifications we need to make.
>

> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-real-quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=107)** Now, continuing on.
>

> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-real-quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=146)** And when the simulation finishes, we can see that each of the possible outcomes for measuring qubit zero occurred about the same number of times.

> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/solution-real-quantum-teleportation?u=76281980&t=154)** That makes sense because we initialized qubit two with a Hadamard gate then teleported that quantum state to qubit zero before measuring it.

> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/your-next-quantum-steps?u=76281980&t=19)** There's still plenty more to learn, so we want to recommend a couple of free resources we've found useful.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/your-next-quantum-steps?u=76281980&t=25)** - We've already mentioned the Qiskit textbook several times throughout this course.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/your-next-quantum-steps?u=76281980&t=29)** It provides a university level course on quantum programming.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/your-next-quantum-steps?u=76281980&t=33)** If you want to dive deeper into math, learn more about gates and algorithms and explore the realities of quantum hardware, this is a great place to go.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/your-next-quantum-steps?u=76281980&t=42)** - Another online resource we've enjoyed is the website, Quantum Country, which hosts several detailed essays about quantum mechanics and computing.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/your-next-quantum-steps?u=76281980&t=50)** It's always helpful to read a different explanation of complex topics like quantum teleportation.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/your-next-quantum-steps?u=76281980&t=55)** - Quantum programming is still a young field, so by the time you watch this course, there will probably be lots of other great resources popping up online.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/your-next-quantum-steps?u=76281980&t=63)** - Thanks for watching this course and having the determination to stick with us to the end.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/quantum-computing-fundamentals/your-next-quantum-steps?u=76281980&t=68)** - [Instructors] Happy programming.
